The Meaning of 'Power to Leave'
Razi answers the fourth objection by clarifying the meaning of 'ability to leave'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's clarification of what it means for God to have the ability to leave an act, and how this answers a specific objection.

Core Idea
Ability to leave means the act was avoidable, not a simultaneous alternative.

In this brief passage, Razi addresses a theological objection regarding the attribute of power (qudra). The objection likely questioned whether God's ability to 'leave' an act implies a change in His power. Razi clarifies that 'ability to leave' simply means that the act could have been left undone, just as it was before. This understanding resolves the objection by showing that the power to leave does not add a new capability but rather describes the same power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi structures his answer: he states the intended meaning of 'qadir ala al-fi'l' and then concludes that this makes the question fall away.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on the terms 'qadir', 'fi'l', and 'tark'. How does Razi define the relationship between them?
3
Understand the logic of the answer
Razi says 'He can do it and He can not do it, rather He leaves it as it was.' Why does saying 'rather He leaves it as it was' resolve the objection? Consider that the objection might be that if God leaves the act, He is not actually doing it, but Razi says the ability to leave is part of the same ability to do.
4
Connect to the broader context
Reflect on why this discussion matters for the doctrine of divine attributes. How does it preserve God's omnipotence without implying change in His essence?
Optional

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن السؤال الرابع: فهو أن المراد من قولنا : إنه قادر على الفعل أنه يمكنه أن يفعل ويمكنه أن لا يفعل بل يتركه كما كان، وعلى هذا والترك هو الوجه يسقط هذا السؤال.
As for the answer to the fourth question: It is that what is meant by our saying 'He is capable of the act' is that He can do it and He can not do it, rather He leaves it as it was. And on this basis, and leaving is the correct view, this question falls away.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This brief passage addresses a fourth objection, likely about the meaning of 'being able to leave the act'. Razi clarifies that when we say God is capable of the act, we mean that He has the ability to do it and also the ability not to do it—that is, He can leave the act undone, just as it was before. This understanding (that 'leaving' is the intended meaning) makes the objection irrelevant.

Key Terms
قادر (qadir)
فعل (fi'l)
ترك (tark)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The ability to leave means that God simultaneously has the power to do and not do an act at the same moment.
Correction: Razi clarifies that 'ability to leave' means that the act could have been left undone, not that God is currently both doing and not doing. It refers to the possibility of the opposite at some point, not simultaneous actuality.

Checkpoint
What does 'qadir ala al-fi'l' mean according to Razi's answer?
A) He must always perform the act
B) He can both do the act and leave it, meaning he could have left it as it was
C) He can only do the act
D) He can only leave the act

Key Takeaway
Razi clarifies that God's ability to leave an act is inherent in His power to do it, and does not imply any change or additional attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray for something, do you truly trust that God's power to 'leave' it undone is just as wise as His power to grant it? How does this shape your patience?
?
If God could have left you uncreated, how does that reality affect your gratitude for His act of creating you—and your purpose in life?
?
He who knows that God's power includes both doing and leaving—does that make you more humble in asking, or more bold? Examine your heart: which one, and why?

Objection Against the Proof and Beginning of Answer
An objection against the proof of divine power using analogy with power and conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that God as a necessitating cause (mujib) can still create at a specific time through conditions and obstacles, and how this parallels the case of divine power (qudra).

Core Idea
Obstacles and conditions can delay the effect of a necessitating cause, just as they affect the exercise of power.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection to the Ash'ari argument that God must be qadir (powerful) rather than mujib (necessitating cause). The objector argues that even if God were a mujib, He could still produce the world at a specific moment if obstacles (like eternity itself) are removed. The argument draws a clever analogy with divine power: just as God is qadir in eternity even though the act's possibility is obstructed by eternity, so too could He be a mujib with a delayed effect. The passage begins to answer by questioning whether a mujib's relation to times is truly equal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the full Arabic and translation. Notice the initial dilemma: either the world's existence in eternity is possible (leading to preeternity) or impossible. The objector takes the second horn.
The objector is not denying the impossibility of an eternal world; instead, he uses it as an opportunity to introduce conditions.
2
Understand the analogy with power
Identify how the objector uses divine power (qudra) as a model. Even though power does not necessitate the act's existence, it necessitates its soundness (sihha). And God is qadir in eternity even when the act's soundness is absent due to the obstacle of eternity.
The Ash'ari response (that power requires absence of obstacle) is turned against them: if that works for power, why not for a mujib?
3
Examine the counter-objection
The text anticipates an objection: if the mujib's relation to all times is equal, then specifying a time would be preference without a preponderator. But the answer begins by noting that they (the Ash'aris) themselves accept time specification for a free agent.
The answer is incomplete; it sets up a parallel argument that will be developed.
4
Ponder the underlying issue
Ask yourself: does this objection successfully challenge the need to posit free will (qudra) over necessitation? The argument shows that a conditioned necessitation (with obstacles) can account for temporal origination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : وجود العالم في الأزل إما أن يكون جائزا أو ممتنعا، فإن كان جائزا فحينئذ يلزم قدم العالم، وعلى هذا التفسير ليس لكم أن تقولوا بأن قدم العالم محال ؛ لأن هذا التقدير هو تقدير أن قدم العالم ليس بمحال وأما إن كان قدم العالم محالا فنقول إن العلة الموجبة قد يتخلف عنها أثرها عند تخلف الشرائط، أو حصول الموانع، ومن أقوى الشرائط كون المعلول في نفسه ممكن الوقوع، ومن أقوى الموانع كونه ممتنع الوقوع فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن الله تعالى موجب بالذات، لوجود العالم، إلا أنه لم يوجد العالم في الأزل؛ لأن تحقق الأزل كالمانع من وجود العالم فلما زال المانع حصل المعلول؟ والذي يحقق هذا السؤال هو أن القدرة، وإن لم تكن موجبة لوجود الفعل عنها، إلا أنها موجبة لصحة وجود الفعل، ثم إنه تعالى قادر في الأزل مع أن صحة الفعل غير حاصلة في الأزل، ولا جواب لكم عن هذا السؤال إلا أن تقولوا : إن القدرة توجب صحة الفعل بشرط عدم المانع، والأزل مانع من هذه الصحة، ولهذا المعنى حصلت القدرة في الأزل مع أنه لم تحصل صحة الفعل في الأزل وإذا صح منكم هذا الجواب في القدر فلم لا يصح مثله في جانب الموجب ؟ ثم نقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إنه تعالى موجب لذاته، وجود العالم في الوقت المخصوص في الأزل؟ وإذا كان كونه موجبًا إنما حصل على هذا الوجه لم يلزم من قدم العلة قدم المعلول. لا يقال : نسبة ذات الموجب إلى جميع الأوقات المقدورة على السوية؛ فاختصاص الإيجاب بذلك الوقت المعين يكون ترجيحا من غير مرجح، وهو محال. لأنا نقول : ألستم تقولون في القادر: إنه صدر عنه فعله في وقت دون وقت لا
If it is said: The existence of the world in eternity is either possible or impossible. If it is possible, then the eternity of the world follows, and on this interpretation you cannot say that the eternity of the world is impossible, because this supposition assumes that the eternity of the world is not impossible. But if the eternity of the world is impossible, then we say that the necessitating cause (al-illah al-mujibah) may have its effect delayed when conditions are absent or obstacles present. Among the strongest conditions is that the effect is in itself possible of occurrence, and among the strongest obstacles is that it is impossible of occurrence. So why is it not permissible to say: God is a mujib by essence for the existence of the world, except that the world did not exist in eternity because the realization of eternity is like an obstacle to the world's existence; when the obstacle ceased, the effect occurred? What substantiates this question is that power (al-qudra), even if it does not necessitate the existence of the act from it, nevertheless necessitates the soundness (sihha) of the act's existence. Then He the Exalted is qadir in eternity even while the soundness of the act is not realized in eternity. And you have no answer to this question except to say: Power necessitates the soundness of the act on condition of the absence of an obstacle, and eternity is an obstacle to this soundness; for this reason, power existed in eternity even though the soundness of the act did not occur in eternity. And if this answer of yours is valid regarding power, why is the like not valid regarding the mujib? Then we say: Why is it not permissible to say: He the Exalted is a mujib by essence, for the existence of the world at a specific time? And if His being a mujib occurs in this way, then the eternity of the cause does not necessitate the eternity of the effect. It cannot be said: The relation of the essence of the mujib to all conceivable times is equal; so the specification of the necessitation (ijab) to that specific time would be a preference without a preponderator, which is impossible. For we say: Do you not say regarding the qadir that His act proceeds from Him at one time and not at another?
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is an objection to Razi's proof that God is qadir. The objector argues that even if God is a mujib, He could still produce the world at a specific time if conditions are met or obstacles removed. For example, if the world's eternity is impossible, then eternity itself acts as an obstacle, and when that obstacle is removed (i.e., when time begins), the world is created. The objector strengthens the point by drawing an analogy with divine power: God is qadir in eternity even though the act is not yet possible (due to the obstacle of eternity), and the defender of free will answers that power only entails soundness of the act on condition of no obstacle. The objector turns this against them: if such a conditional answer works for power, why not for necessitation? Then the objector raises another point: why cannot God be a mujib for a specific time? The text then transitions to a counter-objection about the equal relation of the mujib to all times, and the author begins to answer by pointing out that in the case of a free agent, the same question of time specification arises and is answered by will; similarly, in the case of a mujib, the specification could be due to the condition/obstacle. The answer is incomplete, but the argument is that if they accept time specification for a free agent, they should accept it for a mujib.

Key Terms
موجب
قادر
مانع
شرط
صحة الفعل
ترجيح بلا مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A necessitating cause (mujib) cannot produce an effect at a specific time because it would require a preponderator, which is impossible for God.
Correction: The passage shows that a mujib can produce an effect at a specific time if conditions are met or obstacles removed. The obstacle (e.g., eternity) serves as the preponderator. This is analogous to divine power, which also requires absence of obstacles for the act's soundness.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the objector use to argue that a mujib can produce an effect at a specific time due to removal of an obstacle?
A) The sun's illumination being blocked by clouds.
B) Fire needing oxygen to burn.
C) Divine power existing in eternity even though the act is not yet possible due to an obstacle.
D) A builder constructing a house at a specific time.

Key Takeaway
A necessitating cause can have its effect delayed by obstacles, just as divine power requires the absence of obstacles for an act to be sound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's perfect timing is not a sign of weakness but of His wisdom in arranging conditions and removing obstacles? When you face a delay in your life, do you trust that He is working through hidden conditions, or do you doubt His power? Commit to thanking Him for the delay today.
?
If divine power can be conditioned by obstacles, what does that reveal about your own limited understanding of God's sovereignty? Are you willing to accept that some 'delays' in your duas are actually protective obstacles that God in His mercy places? Ask yourself: What obstacle might be a sign of His care for you?
?
The objection argues that the same logic used for power applies to necessity. Are you consistent in your reasoning about God? Do you sometimes hold God to a standard of simplicity that you would not apply to your own understanding of human action? Repent of any intellectual arrogance and humble yourself before the depth of divine wisdom.

First Horn: Unconditional Causation
Razi presents the first horn of his dilemma on necessary causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why unconditional necessary causation leads to the absurd conclusion that either the world is eternal or God is temporal.

Core Idea
Unconditional necessary causation forces either an eternal world or a temporal God.

Razi begins his refutation by considering the first possibility: that God's effect on the world is unconditional. This leads to two unacceptable consequences, which he presents as a powerful argument against the notion of God as a necessary agent. Let's examine these consequences and see why they are considered absurd in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi sets up the conditional: 'If it is not conditional upon a condition...'
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two consequences
Razi says that from the pre-eternity of the essence would follow the pre-eternity of the world, or from the temporality of the world would follow the temporality of God. Identify both.
The first consequence assumes God is eternal; the second assumes the world is temporal.
3
Understand why both are false
In Islamic theology, God is eternal and the world is temporal. So an eternal world contradicts the world's temporality, and a temporal God contradicts God's eternity. Both are impossible.
This step connects to core_idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالذات لكان تأثيره في وجود العالم إما أن لا يكون موقوفا على شرط وإما أن يكون موقوفا على شرط، فإن لم يكن موقوفًا على شرط لزم من قدمه قدم العالم، أو من حدوث العالم حدوثه، وكلاهما باطلان، وأما إن كان موقوفاً على شرط فذلك الشرط إن كان قديما لزم أيضًا قدم العالم، وإن كان حادثا كان الكلام فيه كما في الأول، فيفضي إلى التسلسل، وهو أن يكون كل حادث مسبوقا بحادث آخر قبله، وذلك قول بحوادث لا أول لها، وقد أبطلناه في مسألة حدوث الأجسام، فثبت أن القول بكونه تعالى موجبًا بالذات يفضي إلى هذه الأقسام الباطلة فيكون باطلاً، وإذا بطل هذا ثبت أنه تعالی قادر فاعل مختار
By essence, if His effect in the existence of the world is either not conditional upon a condition or is conditional upon a condition. If it is not conditional upon a condition, then from its pre-eternity (qidam) would follow the pre-eternity of the world, or from its temporality (huduth) would follow the temporality of the world, and both are false. And if it is conditional upon a condition, then that condition, if it is pre-eternal, would also necessitate the pre-eternity of the world, and if it is temporal, then the discussion about it is as in the first, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul), which is that every temporal event is preceded by another temporal event before it, and that is the assertion of temporal events with no beginning, which we have already refuted in the issue of the temporality of bodies. So it is established that the assertion that God is mujib bi-dhat leads to these false divisions, therefore it is false; and when this is refuted, it is established that God is a powerful, free agent (fa'il mukhtar).
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a proof by dilemma. Razi is refuting the claim that God is a necessary agent (mujib bi-dhat) whose causation is necessitated by His essence. He argues that if God's effect on the world is by essence, then either it is unconditional or conditional. If unconditional, then from the pre-eternity of the essence would follow the pre-eternity of the world, or from its temporality would follow the temporality of the world—both false because the world is temporal and God is eternal. If conditional, then the condition itself must be examined: if pre-eternal, the world would be eternal; if temporal, then that temporal condition itself requires another condition, leading to an infinite regress of temporal events. Razi notes that this regress (tasalsul) has already been refuted in the discussion on the temporality of bodies. Therefore, all divisions lead to false conclusions, so the original claim is false. Hence, God must be a powerful free agent who acts by will and choice.

Key Terms
mujib bi-dhat
qidam
huduth

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessary causation (mujib bi-dhat) is the same as free agency.
Correction: Necessary causation implies a deterministic, unconditional effect, which leads to absurdities about God's eternity and the world's temporality. Free agency (fa'il mukhtar) allows for conditional, will-based action.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, if God's effect were unconditional, which of the following is NOT a possible consequence?
A) The world would be eternal.
B) God would be temporal.
C) The world would be temporal.
D) Both A and B are possible.

Key Takeaway
Unconditional necessary causation forces an impossible choice between an eternal world and a temporal God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that God's actions are mechanical and unconditional, as if He must act in a certain way without any wisdom or condition? How does Razi's dilemma challenge you to see God's actions as deliberate and purposeful? Take a moment to repent from any view that reduces God to a necessary force.
?
Razi shows that unconditional causation leads to contradictions. In your own life, where have you fallen into thinking that your own actions or circumstances are determined without any room for divine wisdom or human choice? How can this lesson help you trust God's wise conditioning of events?
?
This passage refutes the idea that God's essence forces the world's existence. Does your view of God allow for His free will and sovereignty? Examine your heart: Do you truly believe God could have chosen not to create, or do you secretly think creation was necessary? Let this lesson deepen your awe of God's absolute freedom and power.

Second Horn: Conditional Causation and Infinite Regress
Razi completes his dilemma by analyzing the conditional branch and concluding the theory is false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why conditional necessary causation leads to an infinite regress of temporal events, which is false, and how this refutes the idea of God as a necessary agent.

Core Idea
Conditional causation collapses into an infinite regress of conditions, which is impossible.

Having rejected the unconditional branch, Razi now examines the second possibility: that God's effect is conditional. He shows that this leads to an infinite regress of temporal events, a conclusion already refuted in discussions on the temporality of bodies. Thus, the entire theory of necessary agency is false, and only free agency remains.

Guided Path
1
Read the conditional branch passage
Read the Arabic text and translation. Focus on the two possibilities for the condition: eternal or temporal.
Razi uses the same structure as before.
2
Analyze the condition being eternal
If the condition is eternal, then the world would be eternal, which is false for the same reason as before.
This is similar to the unconditional case.
3
Analyze the condition being temporal
If the condition is temporal, then that condition itself requires another condition, leading to an infinite chain of temporal events (tasalsul). Razi says this has been refuted earlier.
Infinite regress of temporal events is impossible because it would imply an unending series with no beginning.
4
Conclude: original claim false
Since both possibilities lead to falsehood, the original claim that God is a necessary agent is false. Therefore, God is a free agent (fa'il mukhtar).
This is the final conclusion of the whole argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالذات لكان تأثيره في وجود العالم إما أن لا يكون موقوفا على شرط وإما أن يكون موقوفا على شرط، فإن لم يكن موقوفًا على شرط لزم من قدمه قدم العالم، أو من حدوث العالم حدوثه، وكلاهما باطلان، وأما إن كان موقوفاً على شرط فذلك الشرط إن كان قديما لزم أيضًا قدم العالم، وإن كان حادثا كان الكلام فيه كما في الأول، فيفضي إلى التسلسل، وهو أن يكون كل حادث مسبوقا بحادث آخر قبله، وذلك قول بحوادث لا أول لها، وقد أبطلناه في مسألة حدوث الأجسام، فثبت أن القول بكونه تعالى موجبًا بالذات يفضي إلى هذه الأقسام الباطلة فيكون باطلاً، وإذا بطل هذا ثبت أنه تعالی قادر فاعل مختار
By essence, if His effect in the existence of the world is either not conditional upon a condition or is conditional upon a condition. If it is not conditional upon a condition, then from its pre-eternity (qidam) would follow the pre-eternity of the world, or from its temporality (huduth) would follow the temporality of the world, and both are false. And if it is conditional upon a condition, then that condition, if it is pre-eternal, would also necessitate the pre-eternity of the world, and if it is temporal, then the discussion about it is as in the first, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul), which is that every temporal event is preceded by another temporal event before it, and that is the assertion of temporal events with no beginning, which we have already refuted in the issue of the temporality of bodies. So it is established that the assertion that God is mujib bi-dhat leads to these false divisions, therefore it is false; and when this is refuted, it is established that God is a powerful, free agent (fa'il mukhtar).
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a proof by dilemma. Razi is refuting the claim that God is a necessary agent (mujib bi-dhat) whose causation is necessitated by His essence. He argues that if God's effect on the world is by essence, then either it is unconditional or conditional. If unconditional, then from the pre-eternity of the essence would follow the pre-eternity of the world, or from its temporality would follow the temporality of the world—both false because the world is temporal and God is eternal. If conditional, then the condition itself must be examined: if pre-eternal, the world would be eternal; if temporal, then that temporal condition itself requires another condition, leading to an infinite regress of temporal events. Razi notes that this regress (tasalsul) has already been refuted in the discussion on the temporality of bodies. Therefore, all divisions lead to false conclusions, so the original claim is false. Hence, God must be a powerful free agent who acts by will and choice.

Key Terms
mujib bi-dhat
tasalsul
fa'il mukhtar

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An infinite regress of temporal events is possible and poses no problem.
Correction: An infinite regress of temporal events (tasalsul) is impossible because it implies no beginning, which contradicts the temporality of events. Razi says it has been refuted in the discussion on the temporality of bodies.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does a temporal condition lead to?
A) The world being eternal
B) An infinite regress of temporal events
C) God becoming temporal
D) The condition being unnecessary

Key Takeaway
The conditional branch of necessary causation collapses into an impossible infinite regress, proving that God must be a free agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself explaining events in your life as mere results of prior causes (circumstances, people, habits), forgetting that behind every cause stands a free and willing Creator? Will you today consciously attribute every detail of your day to His direct will, not to impersonal necessity?
?
When you face trials, do you ever feel that God must act in a certain way to be consistent, as if He were bound by necessity? Does this lesson challenge that assumption and call you to trust in His absolute freedom, even when His actions seem inexplicable?
?
You have just seen that the only coherent position is that God is a free agent. Are you living as if every moment of your existence is a personal act of God's choice? What would change in your worship, your gratitude, and your patience if you truly believed that nothing in your life is a mere byproduct of an impersonal chain?

Divine Attributes

5 min
The Hindrance Argument for a Necessary Cause
The objector presents a dilemma and proposes hindrance as a solution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's argument that a necessary cause can be delayed by a hindrance, specifically that pre-eternity (azal) can serve as a hindrance to the world's existence.

Core Idea
A necessary cause may be delayed by a hindrance — but can pre-eternity itself be that hindrance?

The objector begins by framing the options: either the world's existence in pre-eternity is possible or impossible. If possible, that would imply the world's pre-eternity, which the Ash'ari deny. If impossible, then a necessary cause can be delayed when a hindrance is present. The objector suggests that pre-eternity itself acts as a hindrance, so God can be a necessary agent (mujib bi-dhat) yet the world is temporal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the objector's own logic come through.
2
Identify the two possibilities
Notice the dilemma: if the world's existence in pre-eternity is possible, then the world is pre-eternal. If impossible, then a necessary cause can be delayed.
The objector assumes the second horn to salvage necessary agency.
3
Understand the concept of hindrance
The objector argues that pre-eternity (azal) is a hindrance that prevents the effect from occurring until the hindrance is removed.
This is a key move: treating pre-eternity as a condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : وجود العالم في الأزل إما أن يكون جائزا أو ممتنعا، فإن كان جائزا فحينئذ يلزم قدم العالم، وعلى هذا التفسير ليس لكم أن تقولوا بأن قدم العالم محال ؛ لأن هذا التقدير هو تقدير أن قدم العالم ليس بمحال وأما إن كان قدم العالم محالا فنقول إن العلة الموجبة قد يتخلف عنها أثرها عند تخلف الشرائط، أو حصول الموانع، ومن أقوى الشرائط كون المعلول في نفسه ممكن الوقوع، ومن أقوى الموانع كونه ممتنع الوقوع فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن الله تعالى موجب بالذات، لوجود العالم، إلا أنه لم يوجد العالم في الأزل؛ لأن تحقق الأزل كالمانع من وجود العالم فلما زال المانع حصل المعلول؟ والذي يحقق هذا السؤال هو أن القدرة، وإن لم تكن موجبة لوجود الفعل عنها، إلا أنها موجبة لصحة وجود الفعل، ثم إنه تعالى قادر في الأزل مع أن صحة الفعل غير حاصلة في الأزل، ولا جواب لكم عن هذا السؤال إلا أن تقولوا : إن القدرة توجب صحة الفعل بشرط عدم المانع، والأزل مانع من هذه الصحة، ولهذا المعنى حصلت القدرة في الأزل مع أنه لم تحصل صحة الفعل في الأزل وإذا صح منكم هذا الجواب في القدر فلم لا يصح مثله في جانب الموجب ؟ ثم نقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إنه تعالى موجب لذاته، وجود العالم في الوقت المخصوص في الأزل؟ وإذا كان كونه موجبًا إنما حصل على هذا الوجه لم يلزم من قدم العلة قدم المعلول. لا يقال : نسبة ذات الموجب إلى جميع الأوقات المقدورة على السوية؛ فاختصاص الإيجاب بذلك الوقت المعين يكون ترجيحا من غير مرجح، وهو محال. لأنا نقول : ألستم تقولون في القادر: إنه صدر عنه فعله في وقت دون وقت لا لمرجح أصلا، مع أن نسبة صلاحية القدرة والإرادة بالنسبة إلى كل الأوقات على السوية ؟ فلم لا يجوز مثله في الموجب بالذات ؟ والجواب عن الأول ثبت أن الأزل مناف لحدوث العالم، ولكن إذا كان العالم محدثًا كان حدوثه مختصا بوقت معين ولو كان حادثاً قبل أن حدث بتقدير عشرة أيام لم يصر بهذا القدر أزلياً، وإذا كان كذلك فلا وقت يفرض حدوثه فيه إلا وكان المانع - وهو الأزل - زائلا قبل ذلك الوقت، وإذا كان المانع زائلا قبل ذلكم الوقت وكانت العلة الموجبة حاصلة قبل ذلك لزم حدوثه قبل أن حدث، وذلك محال، فوجب القول بأنه تعالى فاعل بالاختيار لا أنه موجب بالذات. وقوله ثانيا : " لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه تعالى موجب لذاته وقوع العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين؟ قلنا : إذن على هذا التقدير يكون تأثير ذات الله تعالى في وجود العالم مشروطا بحضور ذلك الوقت، وعند هذا يعود القسم الذي ذكرناه من أن ذلك الشرط إن كان قديما لزم قدم المعلول، وإن حادثا كان الكلام فيه كما في الأول، وهذا يقتضي اشتراط كل حادث يحادث آخر، لا إلى أول، وهذا هو القول بوجود حوادث لا أول لها، وقد أبطلناه.
The objection: The existence of the world in pre-eternity (azal) is either possible or impossible. If it is possible, then the pre-eternity of the world would follow, and on this interpretation you cannot say that the pre-eternity of the world is impossible, because this supposition is the supposition that the pre-eternity of the world is not impossible. And if the pre-eternity of the world is impossible, then we say that the necessary cause (al-illa al-mujiba) may have its effect delayed when conditions are absent or hindrances occur, and among the strongest conditions is that the effect in itself is possible of occurrence, and among the strongest hindrances is that it is impossible of occurrence. So why is it not permissible to say: God is mujib bi-dhat for the existence of the world, except that the world did not exist in pre-eternity because the reality of pre-eternity (azal) acts as a hindrance to the existence of the world; when the hindrance ceased, the effect occurred? What confirms this question is that power (qudra), although it does not necessitate the existence of the act from it, it does necessitate the soundness (sihha) of the existence of the act. Then God is powerful (qadir) in pre-eternity while the soundness of the act is not attained in pre-eternity. And you have no answer to this question except to say: Power necessitates the soundness of the act on condition of the absence of a hindrance, and pre-eternity is a hindrance to this soundness. For this reason, power obtains in pre-eternity while the soundness of the act does not obtain in pre-eternity. And if this answer is valid from you regarding power, then why is not the like valid regarding the side of the necessary agent (mujib)? Then we say: Why is it not permissible to say: He, exalted, is mujib bi-dhat for the existence of the world at the specific time in pre-eternity? And if His being mujib only obtains in this way, then the pre-eternity of the cause does not necessitate the pre-eternity of the effect. It cannot be said: The relation of the essence of the necessary agent to all possible times is equal; so the specification of necessitation (ijab) to that specific time would be a preponderance without a preponderater, and that is impossible. For we say: Do you not say regarding the powerful agent (qadir): That from him his act issued at one time and not at another without any preponderater at all, while the relation of the suitability (salahiyya) of power and will to all times is equal? So why is the like not permissible regarding the necessary agent by essence? The answer to the first: It is established that pre-eternity is incompatible with the temporality of the world. But if the world is temporal (muhdath), its temporality is specific to a particular time. Even if it were temporal before it happened by a supposition of ten days, it would not become pre-eternal by this amount. And if this is so, there is no time in which its temporality can be supposed except that the hindrance — pre-eternity — was removed before that time. And if the hindrance was removed before that time, and the necessary cause was present before that, then it would necessitate its occurrence before it occurred, and that is impossible. So it must be said that God is a free agent (fa'il bil-ikhtiyar), not a necessary agent by essence. And His saying, secondly: 'Why is it not permissible to say that God is mujib bi-dhat for the occurrence of the world at that specific time?' We say: Then on this supposition, the effect of God's essence on the existence of the world would be conditional upon the presence of that time, and here the division we mentioned returns: that condition, if pre-eternal, necessitates the pre-eternity of the effect, and if temporal, the discussion about it is as in the first. And this necessitates that every temporal event be conditioned by another temporal event, without a first, and this is the assertion of temporal events with no beginning, which we have refuted.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This is a long objection-and-answer passage. The objector tries to salvage the necessary agency theory by introducing conditions and hindrances. He argues that if the world's existence is impossible in pre-eternity (azal) due to a hindrance, then the necessary cause (illa mujiba) can produce the world later when the hindrance is removed. He also draws an analogy with divine power (qudra), which in the Ash'ari view does not necessitate the act but does necessitate the soundness (sihha) of the act. He claims that just as power exists in pre-eternity without the soundness of the act (due to the hindrance of pre-eternity), so the necessary cause could exist without the effect. Razi's first answer shows that pre-eternity cannot be a hindrance, because if the world is temporal, its temporality is specific to a time; any supposed earlier time would still have had the hindrance removed earlier, forcing the world to occur earlier—an impossibility. In the second answer, Razi shows that if the time itself is a condition, the same dilemma recurs: an eternal condition leads to an eternal effect, a temporal condition leads to infinite regress. Therefore, the only tenable position is that God acts by choice (fa'il bil-ikhtiyar).

Key Terms
azal
illa mujiba
ma'ni'
shart

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That pre-eternity can be a genuine hindrance to divine action.
Correction: Razi will later show that pre-eternity cannot be a hindrance because it leads to the world occurring earlier than it did.

Checkpoint
According to the objector, why can the necessary cause be delayed?
A) Because the cause is weak.
B) Because of the presence of a hindrance or absence of a condition.
C) Because God is not really a necessary agent.
D) Because the world does not yet exist.

Key Takeaway
The objector uses the idea of a hindrance to argue that a necessary cause can produce a temporal effect, but this hinges on treating pre-eternity as a removable obstacle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector blames pre-eternity as a hindrance to God's action. Is there a 'hindrance' in your life—a circumstance, habit, or excuse—that you blame for your failure to act righteously, while in reality you are avoiding the responsibility? What would it take for you to remove that hindrance today?
?
If the objector's argument tempts you to think that God's power is limited by obstacles, examine your own heart: Do you secretly believe that some of God's commands are impossible for you because of your past or present circumstances? How does this passage challenge that belief, and what step will you take to obey despite the perceived hindrance?

The Power Analogy and the Problem of Specification
The objector uses the analogy of power and tackles the specification problem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objector strengthens his case by drawing an analogy with divine power and then raises the specification problem.

Core Idea
If power can exist without its soundness due to a hindrance, why can't a necessary cause exist without its effect?

The objector extends his argument by comparing necessary causation to divine power (qudra). In Ash'ari theology, power does not necessitate the act itself but only its soundness, and power exists in pre-eternity even though soundness is absent due to the hindrance of pre-eternity. The objector claims that the same logic should apply to a necessary cause: it can exist without the effect if a hindrance is present. He then tackles the problem of why the effect occurs at a specific time, arguing that just as a free agent can act at a specific time without a preponderater, so can a necessary agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the logical steps.
2
Understand the power analogy
The objector points out that power necessitates soundness (sihha), not actual existence. Yet power exists in pre-eternity without soundness due to the hindrance of pre-eternity. So why can't a necessary cause exist without its effect similarly?
This is a key analogy that the objector uses to support his case.
3
Examine the specification argument
The objector then says that if God is a necessary agent for the world at a specific time, the problem of specification is no different from the free agent case, where the agent chooses a time without a preponderater.
The objector turns the Ash'ari's own doctrine against them.

The objection: The existence of the world in pre-eternity (azal) is either possible or impossible. If it is possible, then the pre-eternity of the world would follow, and on this interpretation you cannot say that the pre-eternity of the world is impossible, because this supposition is the supposition that the pre-eternity of the world is not impossible. And if the pre-eternity of the world is impossible, then we say that the necessary cause (al-illa al-mujiba) may have its effect delayed when conditions are absent or hindrances occur, and among the strongest conditions is that the effect in itself is possible of occurrence, and among the strongest hindrances is that it is impossible of occurrence. Then God is powerful (qadir) in pre-eternity while the soundness of the act is not attained in pre-eternity. For this reason, power obtains in pre-eternity while the soundness of the act does not obtain in pre-eternity. And if this answer is valid from you regarding power, then why is not the like valid regarding the side of the necessary agent (mujib)? But if the world is temporal (muhdath), its temporality is specific to a particular time. And if the hindrance was removed before that time, and the necessary cause was present before that, then it would necessitate its occurrence before it occurred, and that is impossible. Commentary
This is a long objection-and-answer passage. The objector tries to salvage the necessary agency theory by introducing conditions and hindrances. He argues that if the world's existence is impossible in pre-eternity (azal) due to a hindrance, then the necessary cause (illa mujiba) can produce the world later when the hindrance is removed. He also draws an analogy with divine power (qudra), which in the Ash'ari view does not necessitate the act but does necessitate the soundness (sihha) of the act. He claims that just as power exists in pre-eternity without the soundness of the act (due to the hindrance of pre-eternity), so the necessary cause could exist without the effect. Razi's first answer shows that pre-eternity cannot be a hindrance, because if the world is temporal, its temporality is specific to a time; any supposed earlier time would still have had the hindrance removed earlier, forcing the world to occur earlier—an impossibility. In the second answer, Razi shows that if the time itself is a condition, the same dilemma recurs: an eternal condition leads to an eternal effect, a temporal condition leads to infinite regress. Therefore, the only tenable position is that God acts by choice (fa'il bil-ikhtiyar).

qudra
sihha
qadir
salahiyya
tarjih

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the analogy between power and necessary causation is perfectly parallel.
Correction: Razi will later show that power is not necessary causation, and the analogy fails because power does not necessitate actual existence, while necessary causation does.

Checkpoint
What does the objector claim about power in pre-eternity?
A) Power necessitates the act itself.
B) Power necessitates the soundness of the act, but soundness is absent due to the hindrance of pre-eternity.
C) Power is absent in pre-eternity.
D) Power is identical to the act.

Key Takeaway
The objector uses the power analogy and the free agent's ability to specify a time to argue that a necessary agent can also produce a temporal effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the attribute of God's power, do you ever reduce it to a mechanical force, expecting it to produce effects automatically? How does this argument challenge you to see that God's power can be present even when its effects are not yet manifest?
?
The objector turns the Ash'ari logic against them: if a free agent can specify a time without a reason, why can't a necessary agent? This forces you to examine whether you have ever used a double standard in your own reasoning about God. In what area of your faith might you be accepting a principle for one attribute but denying it for another?
?
You believe God is all-powerful and that His will is free. But do you truly live as though God's power operates without constraint, even when you see delay or hindrance in your own life? What would change if you fully internalized that God's power is not diminished by the absence of immediate effects?

Razi's Refutation: The Impossibility of a Delayed Necessary Cause
Razi refutes the objector's arguments and affirms free agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's two refutations of the hindrance argument and the specification argument, and why only the free agency theory remains.

Core Idea
Pre-eternity cannot be a hindrance; it leads to the world occurring earlier than it did, which is absurd.

Razi presents two decisive answers. First, he shows that if pre-eternity is a hindrance, then for any supposed time of the world's creation, the hindrance would have been removed earlier, forcing the world to occur earlier — an impossibility. Second, he argues that if the time itself is a condition, then either that condition is eternal (leading to eternal effect) or temporal (leading to infinite regress). Thus, only the free agency theory (fa'il bil-ikhtiyar) is coherent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Razi's argument is tight; follow each step.
2
Understand the first answer
Razi shows that pre-eternity cannot be a hindrance because if it were, the world would have occurred earlier than it did, which is impossible.
This refutes the objector's primary argument.
3
Examine the second answer
Razi then considers the objector's specification point. If the time is a condition, then either it is eternal (leading to eternal effect) or temporal (leading to infinite regress). Both are unacceptable.
This closes the door on the necessary agency theory.

فإن قيل : وجود العالم في الأزل إما أن يكون جائزا أو ممتنعا، فإن كان جائزا فحينئذ يلزم قدم العالم، وعلى هذا التفسير ليس لكم أن تقولوا بأن قدم العالم محال ؛ لأن هذا التقدير هو تقدير أن قدم العالم ليس بمحال وأما إن كان قدم العالم محالا فنقول إن العلة الموجبة قد يتخلف عنها أثرها عند تخلف الشرائط، أو حصول الموانع، ومن أقوى الشرائط كون المعلول في نفسه ممكن الوقوع، ومن أقوى الموانع كونه ممتنع الوقوع فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن الله تعالى موجب بالذات، لوجود العالم، إلا أنه لم يوجد العالم في الأزل؛ لأن تحقق الأزل كالمانع من وجود العالم فلما زال المانع حصل المعلول؟ والذي يحقق هذا السؤال هو أن القدرة، وإن لم تكن موجبة لوجود الفعل عنها، إلا أنها موجبة لصحة وجود الفعل، ثم إنه تعالى قادر في الأزل مع أن صحة الفعل غير حاصلة في الأزل، ولا جواب لكم عن هذا السؤال إلا أن تقولوا : إن القدرة توجب صحة الفعل بشرط عدم المانع، والأزل مانع من هذه الصحة، ولهذا المعنى حصلت القدرة في الأزل مع أنه لم تحصل صحة الفعل في الأزل وإذا صح منكم هذا الجواب في القدر فلم لا يصح مثله في جانب الموجب ؟ ثم نقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إنه تعالى موجب لذاته، وجود العالم في الوقت المخصوص في الأزل؟ وإذا كان كونه موجبًا إنما حصل على هذا الوجه لم يلزم من قدم العلة قدم المعلول. لا يقال : نسبة ذات الموجب إلى جميع الأوقات المقدورة على السوية؛ فاختصاص الإيجاب بذلك الوقت المعين يكون ترجيحا من غير مرجح، وهو محال. لأنا نقول : ألستم تقولون في القادر: إنه صدر عنه فعله في وقت دون وقت لا لمرجح أصلا، مع أن نسبة صلاحية القدرة والإرادة بالنسبة إلى كل الأوقات على السوية ؟ فلم لا يجوز مثله في الموجب بالذات ؟ والجواب عن الأول ثبت أن الأزل مناف لحدوث العالم، ولكن إذا كان العالم محدثًا كان حدوثه مختصا بوقت معين ولو كان حادثاً قبل أن حدث بتقدير عشرة أيام لم يصر بهذا القدر أزلياً، وإذا كان كذلك فلا وقت يفرض حدوثه فيه إلا وكان المانع - وهو الأزل - زائلا قبل ذلك الوقت، وإذا كان المانع زائلا قبل ذلكم الوقت وكانت العلة الموجبة حاصلة قبل ذلك لزم حدوثه قبل أن حدث، وذلك محال، فوجب القول بأنه تعالى فاعل بالاختيار لا أنه موجب بالذات. وقوله ثانيا : " لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه تعالى موجب لذاته وقوع العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين؟ قلنا : إذن على هذا التقدير يكون تأثير ذات الله تعالى في وجود العالم مشروطا بحضور ذلك الوقت، وعند هذا يعود القسم الذي ذكرناه من أن ذلك الشرط إن كان قديما لزم قدم المعلول، وإن حادثا كان الكلام فيه كما في الأول، وهذا يقتضي اشتراط كل حادث يحادث آخر، لا إلى أول، وهذا هو القول بوجود حوادث لا أول لها، وقد أبطلناه.
The objection: The existence of the world in pre-eternity (azal) is either possible or impossible. If it is possible, then the pre-eternity of the world would follow, and on this interpretation you cannot say that the pre-eternity of the world is impossible, because this supposition is the supposition that the pre-eternity of the world is not impossible. And if the pre-eternity of the world is impossible, then we say that the necessary cause (al-illa al-mujiba) may have its effect delayed when conditions are absent or hindrances occur, and among the strongest conditions is that the effect in itself is possible of occurrence, and among the strongest hindrances is that it is impossible of occurrence. So why is it not permissible to say: God is mujib bi-dhat for the existence of the world, except that the world did not exist in pre-eternity because the reality of pre-eternity (azal) acts as a hindrance to the existence of the world; when the hindrance ceased, the effect occurred? What confirms this question is that power (qudra), although it does not necessitate the existence of the act from it, it does necessitate the soundness (sihha) of the existence of the act. Then God is powerful (qadir) in pre-eternity while the soundness of the act is not attained in pre-eternity. And you have no answer to this question except to say: Power necessitates the soundness of the act on condition of the absence of a hindrance, and pre-eternity is a hindrance to this soundness. For this reason, power obtains in pre-eternity while the soundness of the act does not obtain in pre-eternity. And if this answer is valid from you regarding power, then why is not the like valid regarding the side of the necessary agent (mujib)? Then we say: Why is it not permissible to say: He, exalted, is mujib bi-dhat for the existence of the world at the specific time in pre-eternity? And if His being mujib only obtains in this way, then the pre-eternity of the cause does not necessitate the pre-eternity of the effect. It cannot be said: The relation of the essence of the necessary agent to all possible times is equal; so the specification of necessitation (ijab) to that specific time would be a preponderance without a preponderater, and that is impossible. For we say: Do you not say regarding the powerful agent (qadir): That from him his act issued at one time and not at another without any preponderater at all, while the relation of the suitability (salahiyya) of power and will to all times is equal? So why is the like not permissible regarding the necessary agent by essence? The answer to the first: It is established that pre-eternity is incompatible with the temporality of the world. But if the world is temporal (muhdath), its temporality is specific to a particular time. Even if it were temporal before it happened by a supposition of ten days, it would not become pre-eternal by this amount. And if this is so, there is no time in which its temporality can be supposed except that the hindrance — pre-eternity — was removed before that time. And if the hindrance was removed before that time, and the necessary cause was present before that, then it would necessitate its occurrence before it occurred, and that is impossible. So it must be said that God is a free agent (fa'il bil-ikhtiyar), not a necessary agent by essence. And His saying, secondly: 'Why is it not permissible to say that God is mujib bi-dhat for the occurrence of the world at that specific time?' We say: Then on this supposition, the effect of God's essence on the existence of the world would be conditional upon the presence of that time, and here the division we mentioned returns: that condition, if pre-eternal, necessitates the pre-eternity of the effect, and if temporal, the discussion about it is as in the first. And this necessitates that every temporal event be conditioned by another temporal event, without a first, and this is the assertion of temporal events with no beginning, which we have refuted.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This is a long objection-and-answer passage. The objector tries to salvage the necessary agency theory by introducing conditions and hindrances. He argues that if the world's existence is impossible in pre-eternity (azal) due to a hindrance, then the necessary cause (illa mujiba) can produce the world later when the hindrance is removed. He also draws an analogy with divine power (qudra), which in the Ash'ari view does not necessitate the act but does necessitate the soundness (sihha) of the act. He claims that just as power exists in pre-eternity without the soundness of the act (due to the hindrance of pre-eternity), so the necessary cause could exist without the effect. Razi's first answer shows that pre-eternity cannot be a hindrance, because if the world is temporal, its temporality is specific to a time; any supposed earlier time would still have had the hindrance removed earlier, forcing the world to occur earlier—an impossibility. In the second answer, Razi shows that if the time itself is a condition, the same dilemma recurs: an eternal condition leads to an eternal effect, a temporal condition leads to infinite regress. Therefore, the only tenable position is that God acts by choice (fa'il bil-ikhtiyar).

Key Terms
fa'il bil-ikhtiyar
tasalsul
azal
mujib bi-dhat

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Razi's first answer, why does the hindrance of pre-eternity fail?
A) Because pre-eternity is not a real hindrance.
B) Because it would require the world to occur earlier than it did, which is impossible.
C) Because the necessary cause cannot be delayed.
D) Because the world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Razi's refutation shows that only the free agency theory avoids absurd consequences like the world occurring earlier or an infinite regress of temporal conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God were a necessary cause, would you feel any less responsible for your actions? Do you ever live as if God's will were mechanical, forgetting that He chooses to create, command, and forgive you at every moment? How would your prayer change if you truly believed God is not compelled?
?
Razi shows that thinking of God as bound by necessity leads to logical absurdities. But more personally: when you face trials, do you ever secretly think God 'had no choice' or that the universe is indifferent? How does the truth that God acts by free will — not fate — affect your trust in His wisdom and mercy right now?
?
The world's existence at this specific time testifies to God's free choice. Look at your own life: are you living as if your existence is a random cosmic accident, or as a deliberate act of a free and loving Creator? What one concrete step can you take today to live in gratitude for that personal act of divine will?

Opponent's First Proof: God as Necessary Agent
Opponent's first proof for absolute necessitarianism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument that God's effecter-ness must be necessary, leading to the conclusion that God is a necessary agent (mujib bi-dhat), and the logical steps used to exclude temporal attributes.

Core Idea
God's effecter-ness must be eternal, so He is a necessary agent.

This lesson presents the first proof offered by the opponent of free will in divine action. The argument attempts to show that God's causation of the world must be necessary, not voluntary, given that it must be by His essence or an eternal attribute. Follow the logical steps carefully, as understanding this argument is essential for grasping the Ash'arite response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the opponent's reasoning come through before interpreting.
2
Identify the three possibilities
The opponent lists three possible bases for God's effecter-ness: by essence, by an eternal attribute, or by a temporal attribute. Notice how these are presented as exhaustive.
3
Understand why the temporal option is rejected
The opponent argues that a temporal attribute would either require no cause (leading to atheism) or require a cause (leading to infinite regress). Both are absurd, so the temporal option is eliminated.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Follow the deduction from permanence to necessity
Since the essence and eternal attribute are permanent, the effecter-ness must also be permanent and necessary. If it were not, the effect would sometimes occur and sometimes not without a preponderater, which is impossible.
The opponent uses the principle of sufficient reason.
5
Connect to the conclusion of necessary agency
Because effecter-ness is necessarily established and unchangeable, God becomes a necessary agent (mujib bi-dhat) rather than a free agent (fa'il bi-ikhtiyar).
This is the point Razi will later refute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج المخالف على قوله بوجوه : الحجة الأولى : لا شك أنه تعالى مؤثر في وجود العالم فكونه مؤثر في العالم إما أن يكون لذاته أو لصفة قديمة أو لصفة محدثة والقسم الثالث باطل؛ لأن تلك الصفة المحدثة إن وقعت لا عن مؤثر لزم نفي الصانع، وإن افتقرت إلى المؤثر لزم التسلسل، ولما بطل هذا القسم تعين أحد القسمين الأولين، وهو أن يكون كونه مؤثرًا في العالم إما لذاته وإما لصفة قديمة، وإذا كانت هذه المؤثرية إوما لأجل الذات وإما لأجل الصفة القديمة بالذات لزم من دوام الذات ودوام تلك الصفة وجوب دوام تلك المؤثرية، إذ لو لم تجب لزم أن يحصل الأثر تارة وأن لا يحصل أخرى، فيكون تمييز إحدى الحالتين عن الأخرى لا لمرجح، وهو محال، وإذا كانت تلك المؤثرية واجبة الثبوت ممتنعة الزوال كان موجبًا بالذات لا فاعلا بالقدرة والاختيار.
The opponent argued against his statement with proofs: The first proof: There is no doubt that God is an effecter (mu'aththir) in the existence of the world. So His being an effecter in the world is either by His essence, or by an eternal attribute, or by a temporal attribute. The third division is false, because that temporal attribute, if it occurred without an effecter, would entail the denial of the Creator (nafy al-sani'), and if it required an effecter, an infinite regress (tasalsul) would follow. And when this division is false, one of the first two divisions is determined: that His being an effecter in the world is either by His essence or by an eternal attribute. And if this effecter-ness (mu'aththiriyya) is either because of the essence or because of the eternal attribute by essence, then from the permanence of the essence and the permanence of that attribute follows the necessity of the permanence of that effecter-ness. For if it were not necessary, it would follow that the effect sometimes occurs and sometimes does not, and this would be a specification of one state over the other without a preponderater, and that is impossible. And if that effecter-ness is necessarily established and impossible of removal, then He is a necessary agent by essence (mujib bi-dhat), not a powerful free agent.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage presents the opponent's first proof for the claim that God is a necessary agent (mujib bi-dhat). The opposer argues that God is clearly an effecter (mu'aththir) of the world. This effecter-ness must be either by God's essence, by an eternal attribute, or by a temporal attribute. The third option is invalid because a temporal attribute itself would need an effecter, leading to infinite regress or atheism. So it must be by essence or an eternal attribute. Since both the essence and an eternal attribute are permanent (daim), the effecter-ness must also be permanent and necessary. If it were not necessary, the effect would sometimes appear and sometimes not, which would require a preponderater for no reason—impossible. Therefore, effecter-ness is eternal and necessary, making God a necessary agent, not a free agent. Note that Razi himself opposes this conclusion, but here he is presenting the argument that he will later refute.

Key Terms
mu'aththir
mu'aththiriyya
mujib bi-dhat
tasalsul
nafy al-sani'
daim

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that if God's efficacy is by His essence, it must be eternal and necessary, which is used by the opponent to argue for necessitarianism.
Correction: The Ash'arite response distinguishes between eternal essential causation and free choice with eternal attributes. The opponent's argument conflates permanence with necessity, but the Ash'arites maintain that God can be eternal yet act freely.

Checkpoint
According to the opponent's first proof, why is the possibility that God's effecter-ness is by a temporal attribute considered false?
A) Because temporal attributes cannot be attributed to God.
B) Because it would lead to infinite regress or denial of the Creator.
C) Because it would make the world eternal.
D) Because temporal attributes require a preponderater.

Key Takeaway
The opponent argues that God's effecter-ness must be necessary, making Him a necessary agent, by eliminating temporality and inferring permanence from the nature of the cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to accept the opponent's conclusion that God is a necessary agent, would your heart still yearn for His personal attention in your duas? How would this view affect your hope in His mercy when you repent? Let this question expose any hidden assumption you might have that God is bound by your actions.
?
The opponent's argument relies on the premise that God's effecter-ness is permanent because His essence is permanent. Do you feel any temptation to think of God's actions as automatic and predictable? How does this temptation clash with the Islamic understanding of God's majesty and free will? In what areas of your life do you need to remind yourself that God is a free agent, not a cosmic machine?

First Proof: Influence by Essence or Eternal Attribute
Opponent argues that God's influence must be eternal, making Him necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument that God's influence must be eternal, making Him a necessitating agent rather than a free agent.

Core Idea
If God causes the world, His influence must be permanent and necessary, not by choice.

This lesson presents a proof from the opponent's perspective in Imam Razi's Al-Arbaeen. The argument aims to show that if God is the influencer of the world, His influence must be eternal and necessary, eliminating the possibility of voluntary choice. Understanding this proof is crucial before Razi's refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Do not try to analyze yet; just let the structure sink in.
Notice the three divisions introduced at the start.
2
Identify the three possible sources of influence
The opponent states that God's influence is either by His essence, an eternal attribute, or a temporal attribute. Identify each in the text.
3
Understand why the temporal attribute is invalid
The opponent gives two reasons: if the temporal attribute occurs without an influencer, it denies the Maker; if it requires an influencer, it leads to infinite regress. Consider why both are unacceptable.
This eliminates the third division.
4
Grasp the conclusion that God is a necessitating agent
With only the first two divisions left, the opponent argues that since the essence and eternal attribute are permanent, their influence must be permanent too. Otherwise, the effect would vary without cause, which is impossible. Thus God is a necessitating agent (mujib bil-dhat), not a free agent.
This is the core conclusion that Razi will refute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : لا شك أنه تعالى مؤثر في وجود العالم فكونه مؤثر في العالم إما أن يكون لذاته أو لصفة قديمة أو لصفة محدثة والقسم الثالث باطل؛ لأن تلك الصفة المحدثة إن وقعت لا عن مؤثر لزم نفي الصانع، وإن افتقرت إلى المؤثر لزم التسلسل، ولما بطل هذا القسم تعين أحد القسمين الأولين، وهو أن يكون كونه مؤثرًا في العالم إما لذاته وإما لصفة قديمة، وإذا كانت هذه المؤثرية إما لأجل الذات وإما لأجل الصفة القديمة بالذات لزم من دوام الذات ودوام تلك الصفة وجوب دوام تلك المؤثرية، إذ لو لم تجب لزم أن يحصل الأثر تارة وأن لا يحصل أخرى، فيكون تمييز إحدى الحالتين عن الأخرى لا لمرجح، وهو محال، وإذا كانت تلك المؤثرية واجبة الثبوت ممتنعة الزوال كان موجبًا بالذات لا فاعلا بالقدرة والاختيار.
The first proof: There is no doubt that He, exalted, is an influencer (mu'aththir) upon the existence of the world. So His being an influencer upon the world is either by His essence (dhat) or by an eternal attribute (sifa qadima) or by a temporal attribute (sifa muhdatha). And the third division is invalid, because that temporal attribute, if it occurs without an influencer, necessitates denial of the Maker; and if it depends on an influencer, it necessitates infinite regress (tasalsul). And since this division is invalid, one of the first two divisions is determined: that His being an influencer upon the world is either by His essence or by an eternal attribute. And if this influencer-ness is either for the sake of the essence or for the sake of the eternal attribute essentially, then from the permanence of the essence and the permanence of that attribute follows the necessity of the permanence of that influencer-ness. For if it were not necessary, it would entail that the effect occurs sometimes and does not occur at other times, then distinguishing one of the two states from the other would be without a preponderant cause (murajjih), which is impossible. And if that influencer-ness is necessarily established and impossible to cease, then He is a necessitating agent (mujib bil-dhat), not an agent by power (fa'il bil-qudra) and choice.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage presents the opponent's first proof. The opponent argues that God is undoubtedly an influencer (mu'aththir) on the world's existence. This influence must be due either to His essence, an eternal attribute, or a temporal attribute. The third option (temporal attribute) is invalid for two reasons: if the temporal attribute occurs without an influencer, that denies the Maker; if it requires an influencer, that leads to infinite regress. Thus the influence must be by essence or an eternal attribute. Since both the essence and the attribute are eternal and permanent, their influence must also be permanent and necessary. Otherwise, the effect would occur sometimes and not others, requiring a preponderant cause, which is impossible. Therefore, the influence is necessary and unceasing, making God a necessitating agent (mujib bil-dhat), not a free agent by power and choice. This proof is designed to force the conclusion that if God is the cause of the world, He must be a necessary agent, contradicting the claim that He acts by choice.

Key Terms
مؤثر
ذات
صفة قديمة
صفة محدثة
تسلسل
مرجح
موجب بالذات
فاعل بالقدرة والاختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this proof represents Razi's own position.
Correction: This is the opponent's proof; Razi will refute it later in the work.

Checkpoint

A) Because a temporal attribute would require a preponderant cause.
B) Because it either denies the Maker or leads to infinite regress.
C) Because a temporal attribute cannot exist.
D) Because it would make God's essence temporal.

Key Takeaway
The opponent argues that since God's influence cannot be temporal, it must be permanent and necessary, making God a necessitating agent rather than a voluntary agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that God must be a necessitating agent if His influence is permanent, did you feel a tension with your belief that God acts by will and choice? What does that tension reveal about how you understand divine freedom? Are you willing to trust that God's actions are not compelled, even if logic seems to suggest otherwise?
?
If this proof were true, your heartfelt du'as would be meaningless—God could not choose to respond differently. Does that possibility shake your reliance on Allah? How does your certainty in God's free will shape the way you pray and seek His mercy today?
?
The opponent eliminates a temporal attribute by showing it leads to infinite regress. In your own life, do you ever rely on secondary causes as if they are ultimate, forgetting that Allah alone is the independent influencer? Examine one habit or reliance that you treat as automatic—how does this lesson call you to re-center your trust in Allah's direct, voluntary action?

The Contradiction in Attributing Power to God
Opponent argues that ascribing power to God leads to contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument that ascribing power to God leads to a logical contradiction, and how it challenges the coherence of divine power.

Core Idea
Divine power leads to contradiction because the object's distinction must be both prior and posterior to power.

This passage presents a sophisticated philosophical argument against the coherence of divine power. The opponent contends that the very concept of a powerful being (qadir) acting upon an object of power (maqdur) leads to an impossible circularity. By examining the prerequisites of power, we uncover a deep problem: the object's distinction from impossibles must both precede and follow the power's engagement. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the literal translation. Let the opponent's argument unfold naturally.
The argument is dense; don't get bogged down in the first reading.
2
Identify the contradiction claim
The opponent claims that for power to be over something, that thing must be distinct from impossibles prior to power's attachment. But the object's existence (and thus its distinction) is only realized after power's effect. This creates a requirement for both priority and posteriority.
Notice the two premises: (1) distinction must be prior, (2) distinction must be posterior.
3
Examine the objection and rebuttal
The opponent anticipates an objection based on the quiddity (mahiyya) being prior while existence is posterior. The rebuttal argues that if quiddity is unaffected by power, then it cannot be the object of power; only existence or the act of describing quiddity with existence can be. This brings back the same circularity.
This shows how the argument handles a common escape route.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider what this argument implies for theistic philosophy. If successful, it would show that the very notion of divine power is incoherent. Think about how a defender of divine power might respond.
This argument is an opponent's proof, not the author's own view.

Source Text

الحجة الثانية : القول يكون المؤثر قادراً يفضي إلى التناقض، فيكون القول به باطلا . إنما قلنا : إنه يفضي إلى التناقض لأن كون القادر على المقدور موقوف على تميز ذلك المقدور في نفسه من الممتنعات؛ لأنه لولا ذلك التميز لم تكن قدرته عليها أولى من قدرته على الممتنعات وهذا يقتضي أن يكون تميز المقدور عن غيره سابقا على تعلق قدرة القادرية. وأيضًا: المقدور هو الذي يقع بتأثر القادر وتكوينه، وهذا يقتضي أن يكون تحقق ذات المقدور متأخرًا عن تعلق قدرة القادرية، وإذا كان تحقق ذاته متأخرا عن تعلق قدرة القادرية كان تميزه عن غيره أولى بأن يكون متأخرًا عن تحقق ذاته؛ لأن التميز حكم من أحكام ذاته، وحالة من أحوال ذاته وحكم الشيء وحاله متأخر عن تحقق ذاته وهذا يقتضي أن يكون تميز المقدور عن غيره متقدمًا على تعلق قدرة القادرية، وأن يكون متأخرًا عنه، وذلك محال. فثبت أن القول بكون القادر قادرًا على الشيء يفضي إلى هذا المحال، فكان القول بكون القادر قادرًا على الشيء محالا. لا يقال: ماهية المقدور متقدمة على تعلق القدرة ووجوده متأخر عن تعلق القدرة، كما هو مذهب القائلين بأن المعدوم شيء. لأنا نقول : إذا كانت الماهية متقررة في العدم وفي الوجود، ولا تأثير للقدرة فيها البتة، لم تكن الماهية مقدرة البتة بل كان المقدور إما الوجود وإما جعل الماهية موصوفة بالوجود، وهذا من حيث إن متعلق القدرة يجب أن يكون متقدما على القدرة، ومن حيث إن أثر القدرة يجب أن يكون متأخرًا عن القدرة، فيعود المحال المذكور.
The second proof: The statement that the influencer is powerful (qadir) leads to contradiction, so the statement is invalid. We say: It leads to contradiction because the powerful being's being capable of the object of power (maqdur) depends on the distinction (tamyiz) of that object of power in itself from the impossibles. For without that distinction, its power over it would not be more fitting than its power over impossibles. And this necessitates that the distinction of the object of power from others be prior to the attachment of the power of the powerful one. Also: The object of power is that which occurs by the effect of the powerful one and his making (takwin). And this necessitates that the realization of the essence of the object of power be posterior to the attachment of the power of the powerful one. And if the realization of its essence is posterior to the attachment of the power of the powerful one, then its distinction from others is more fitting to be posterior to the realization of its essence, because distinction is a judgment (hukm) of its essence and a state (hal) of its essence, and the judgment and state of a thing are posterior to the realization of its essence. And this necessitates that the distinction of the object of power from others be prior to the attachment of the power of the powerful one, and also posterior to it, and that is impossible. So it is established that the statement that the powerful is powerful over a thing leads to this impossibility, so the statement that the powerful is powerful over a thing is impossible. It cannot be said: The quiddity (mahiyya) of the object of power is prior to the attachment of power while its existence is posterior to the attachment of power, as is the doctrine of those who say that non-existence is a thing (al-ma'dum shay'). For we say: If the quiddity is established in non-existence and in existence, and power has no effect upon it at all, then the quiddity is not an object of power at all. Rather, the object of power is either existence or the making of the quiddity described by existence. And this is with respect to the fact that the object of power must be prior to power, and with respect to the fact that the effect of power must be posterior to power, so the aforementioned impossibility returns.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage presents the opponent's second proof. The opponent argues that attributing power (qudra) to God leads to a contradiction. The argument proceeds as follows: for the powerful being to be capable of an object of power (maqdur), that object must be distinct (mutamayyiz) from impossible things; otherwise, power would apply equally to impossibles. This distinction must be prior to the attachment of power. However, the object of power is exactly what comes into existence through the power's effect, so its essence is realized after the power's attachment. If its essence is posterior, then its distinction (a judgment on that essence) must also be posterior. Thus the distinction must be both prior and posterior to the power's attachment – an impossibility. Therefore, the very concept of a powerful being having power over something is contradictory. The opponent then anticipates an objection: one might say that the quiddity (mahiyya) of the object is prior, while its existence is posterior, as held by those who consider non-existence as a thing. The opponent rebuts this: if the quiddity is fixed in both non-existence and existence and power has no effect on it, then the quiddity is not the object of power; the object of power is either existence or the act of making the quiddity described by existence. In either case, the same circularity returns: the object must be prior (as the object of power's attachment) and posterior (as the effect of power). Hence the contradiction remains.

Misconception: This argument proves that God cannot be powerful.
Correction: This is an opponent's proof, not the author's own view. It is presented as a challenge to be refuted. The author likely disagrees with this reasoning.

Checkpoint
Why does the opponent claim that ascribing power to God leads to a contradiction?
A) Because power must be eternal, but the object of power is temporal.
B) Because the distinction of the object must be both prior and posterior to the power's attachment.
C) Because power implies choice, which contradicts necessity.
D) Because the object of power cannot be distinguished from impossibles.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's second proof attempts to show that the concept of divine power is self-contradictory, but it is presented as a challenge to be answered, not as the final word.

?

?
