First Proof: Interchangeability of Knowledges
First proof: interchangeability condition of similar things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Husayn's first argument that future and present knowledge cannot be identical because they are not interchangeable.

Core Idea
If two knowledges were identical, they should substitute for each other, but they do not.

Abu al-Husayn begins with a simple test of interchangeability. If two things are truly identical, each should be able to replace the other without changing the situation. He shows that future knowledge and present knowledge fail this test, thereby refuting their identity.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof
Read the Arabic passage for the first proof and its translation. Notice the logical structure: condition of interchangeability, then application to the two knowledges at two different times.
2
Understand the interchangeability condition
The condition for two things being similar (مثلين) is that each can stand in the place of the other (يقوم مقام). Abu al-Husayn claims future and present knowledge do not meet this condition.
This is a conceptual test, not a physical one.
3
Follow the temporal analysis
Compare the two times: before the event and after. Before, believing it will happen is knowledge; believing it has happened is ignorance. After, the opposite holds. Thus, the two beliefs are not interchangeable.
The reversal of truth values shows they are different in essence.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since they are not interchangeable, they are different in reality (حقيقة). Therefore, claiming identity is impossible.
The proof relies on a straightforward logical test.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أبو الحسين البصري فقال: هذا المذهب باطل، ويمتنع أن يقال: بأن العلم بأن الشيء سيوجد هو نفس العلم بوجوده حال ما يصير موجوداً، وله أن يحتج على ذلك بوجوه : الحجة الأولى : أن من شرط المثلين أن يقوم كل واحد منهما مقام الآخر، والعلم بأن الشيء سيوجد لا يقوم مقام العلم بأنه موجود الآن، فإن قبل وقوع المعلوم لو اعتقدنا بأنه سيقع بعد ذلك، كان علماً، ولو اعتقدنا أنه واقع الآن كان جهلاً، وأما حال وقوعه فإنه ينقلب الأمر ، فلو اعتقدنا بأنه سيقع بعد ذلك وأنه الآن غير واقع كان جهلا ، ولو اعتقدنا أنه الآن واقع كان علمًا ، فثبت أن كل واحد منهما لا يقوم مقام الآخر، وذلك يقتضي كون هذين الاعتقادين مختلفين في الحقيقة، ومع هذا الاختلاف في الماهية والحقيقة كيف يمكن دعوى الاتحاد ؟ الحجة الثانية : أن كونه عالمًا بأنه سيقع غير مشروط بكونه واقعا في الحال، وكونه عالمًا بوقوعه مشروط بوقوعه في الحال والشيئان اللذان يكون أحدهما مشروطا بشيء والآخر لا يكون مشروطًا بذلك الشيء يمتنع أن يكون أحدهما نفس الآخر. الحجة الثالثة : وهي التي عول عليها أبو الحسين فقال : مجرد العلم بأن الشيء سيقع، لا يكون علمًا بوقوعه إذا وقع، فإن من علم أن زيدا سيدخل البلد غدًا ثم إنه جلس في بيت مظلم لا يميز فيه بين الليل والنهار وبقي مستديما لذلك العلم حتى جاء الغد ودخل زيد البلد فههنا هذا الشخص بمجرد علمه بأن زيدا سيدخل البلد غدًا لا عالمًا بأنه دخل الآن في البلد، فثبت بهذا أن العلم بأن الشيء سيوجد غدًا لا يصير يكون نفس العلم بوجوده إذا وجد. بلى من علم أن زيدا سيدخل البلد غدًا ثم علم حصول الغد فحينئذ يتولد من هذين العلمين علم ثالث وهو العلم بأن زيدا دخل البلد الآن. الحجة الرابعة : أن العلم بالشيء صورة مطابقة لذلك الشيء ولا شك أن حقيقة الآن غير واقع - مغايرة لحقيقة أنه واقع في الحال وحاصل، وأنه سيقع بعد ذلك - وإذا اختلفت المعلومات وجب اختلاف العلمين. الحجة الخامسة : وهو أنه يمكننا أن نعلم كونه عالما بأن الشيء الفلاني سيقع غدا حال ما نجهل كونه عالما بوقوعه حال وقوعه ولما حصل العلم بأحد هذين العلمين حال ما حصل الشك في حصول العلم الآخر علمنا تغاير العلم.
As for Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, he said: This view is false, and it is impossible to say that knowledge that something will exist is the same knowledge as its existence when it comes to exist. He has to argue for that in several ways: The first proof: that from the condition of two similar things is that each one takes the place of the other, and knowledge that something will exist does not take the place of knowledge that it exists now. For before the occurrence of the known, if we believed that it will occur later, it is knowledge, and if we believed that it is real now, it is ignorance. As for the time of its occurrence, the matter is reversed: if we believed that it will occur later and that it is now not real, it is ignorance, and if we believed that it is now real, it is knowledge. So it is established that neither takes the place of the other, and that necessitates that these two beliefs are different in reality. With this difference in essence and reality, how can the claim of identity be possible? The second proof: that His being knowledgeable that it will occur is not conditioned on its being real in the present, and His being knowledgeable of its occurrence is conditioned on its occurrence in the present. Two things such that one is conditioned by something and the other is not conditioned by that something are impossible to be one the same as the other. The third proof: which is the one Abu al-Husayn relied on, he said: mere knowledge that something will occur is not knowledge of its occurrence when it occurs. For if someone knows that Zayd will enter the city tomorrow, then he sits in a dark room where he cannot distinguish between night and day, and he continues to hold that knowledge until morning comes and Zayd enters the city, then here this person by mere knowledge that Zayd will enter the city tomorrow is not knowing that he has now entered the city. Thus it is established that knowledge that something will exist tomorrow does not become the same knowledge as its existence when it exists. Indeed, if someone knows that Zayd will enter the city tomorrow and then knows the arrival of tomorrow, then from these two knowledges a third knowledge is generated, which is knowledge that Zayd has now entered the city. The fourth proof: that knowledge of a thing is a form corresponding to that thing, and there is no doubt that the reality of 'now not real' is different from the reality of 'it is real in the present and actual', and that 'it will occur later'. If the known objects differ, then the two knowledges must differ. The fifth proof: that we can know that He is knowing that a certain thing will occur tomorrow while we are ignorant of His being knowing of its occurrence at the time of its occurrence, and since knowledge of one of these two knowledges occurs while doubt occurs about the other knowledge, we know the two knowledges are distinct.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage presents Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's five proofs against the majority view that future knowledge is identical to present knowledge. Each proof highlights a logical or conditional difference between the two. The first proof uses substitution: before an event, believing it will happen is knowledge; believing it has happened is ignorance. After the event, the opposite is true. The two are not interchangeable, so they are distinct. The second proof: future knowledge is not conditioned on actual occurrence; present knowledge is. Things with different conditions cannot be identical. The third proof uses an example of a person in a dark room: knowledge of future entry does not automatically give knowledge of present entry; a third knowledge is generated from the future knowledge and the knowledge that time has elapsed. The fourth proof: knowledge is a form corresponding to the known; since the known (future-as-not-yet-real vs. present-as-real) differ, knowledge differs. The fifth proof: we can know God knows a future event while not knowing whether He knows it has occurred, indicating they are distinct. Together, these proofs aim to refute the identity claim and support Abu al-Husayn's position.

Key Terms
المثلين
يقوم مقام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a belief persists over time, it must be the same knowledge.
Correction: Abu al-Husayn shows that the truth value of the belief changes with time, indicating different knowledges.

Checkpoint
What does Abu al-Husayn's first proof use as a criterion for two things being similar?
A) They must have the same subject.
B) Each must take the place of the other.
C) They must be about the same object.
D) They must occur at the same time.

Key Takeaway
Future and present knowledge fail the interchangeability test, so they are not identical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you anticipate a future event, do you treat that expectation with the same certainty as your knowledge of what is happening right now? How does this proof expose a flaw in that assumption?
?
You have likely heard that God's knowledge encompasses all times equally. Does this logical distinction between future and present knowledge unsettle your mental image of divine omniscience? What will you do with that discomfort?
?
This proof shows that even in human reasoning, we must distinguish between types of knowledge that seem similar. Where in your spiritual life have you been conflating different kinds of certainty—for example, hope with faith, or assumption with conviction? What would it look like to honor those distinctions honestly?

Second Proof: Conditionality of Present Knowledge
Second proof: conditionality of present knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Husayn's second argument that future knowledge and present knowledge differ because one is conditioned on actual occurrence while the other is not.

Core Idea
Different conditions imply different realities.

The second proof examines the conditions attached to each type of knowledge. Present knowledge requires the event to be actually occurring, while future knowledge does not. This conditional difference shows they cannot be identical.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Read the Arabic and translation for the second proof. Note the two statements about conditionality.
2
Identify the conditionality difference
Knowledge of future occurrence is not conditioned on the event being present (ghair mashrut), while knowledge of present occurrence is conditioned on its being present (mashrut).
Condition here means a necessary prerequisite for the truth of the knowledge.
3
Apply the logical principle
If two things differ in conditionality, they cannot be identical. Abu al-Husayn states this as a principle.
This is a tautological truth: if X requires condition C and Y does not, X and Y are distinct.
4
Conclude the distinction
Therefore, future and present knowledge are distinct.
The proof is concise and relies on a basic logical rule.

Source Text

وأما أبو الحسين البصري فقال: هذا المذهب باطل، ويمتنع أن يقال: بأن العلم بأن الشيء سيوجد هو نفس العلم بوجوده حال ما يصير موجوداً، وله أن يحتج على ذلك بوجوه : الحجة الأولى : أن من شرط المثلين أن يقوم كل واحد منهما مقام الآخر، والعلم بأن الشيء سيوجد لا يقوم مقام العلم بأنه موجود الآن، فإن قبل وقوع المعلوم لو اعتقدنا بأنه سيقع بعد ذلك، كان علماً، ولو اعتقدنا أنه واقع الآن كان جهلاً، وأما حال وقوعه فإنه ينقلب الأمر ، فلو اعتقدنا بأنه سيقع بعد ذلك وأنه الآن غير واقع كان جهلا ، ولو اعتقدنا أنه الآن واقع كان علمًا ، فثبت أن كل واحد منهما لا يقوم مقام الآخر، وذلك يقتضي كون هذين الاعتقادين مختلفين في الحقيقة، ومع هذا الاختلاف في الماهية والحقيقة كيف يمكن دعوى الاتحاد ؟ الحجة الثانية : أن كونه عالمًا بأنه سيقع غير مشروط بكونه واقعا في الحال، وكونه عالمًا بوقوعه مشروط بوقوعه في الحال والشيئان اللذان يكون أحدهما مشروطا بشيء والآخر لا يكون مشروطًا بذلك الشيء يمتنع أن يكون أحدهما نفس الآخر. الحجة الثالثة : وهي التي عول عليها أبو الحسين فقال : مجرد العلم بأن الشيء سيقع، لا يكون علمًا بوقوعه إذا وقع، فإن من علم أن زيدا سيدخل البلد غدًا ثم إنه جلس في بيت مظلم لا يميز فيه بين الليل والنهار وبقي مستديما لذلك العلم حتى جاء الغد ودخل زيد البلد فههنا هذا الشخص بمجرد علمه بأن زيدا سيدخل البلد غدًا لا عالمًا بأنه دخل الآن في البلد، فثبت بهذا أن العلم بأن الشيء سيوجد غدًا لا يصير يكون نفس العلم بوجوده إذا وجد. بلى من علم أن زيدا سيدخل البلد غدًا ثم علم حصول الغد فحينئذ يتولد من هذين العلمين علم ثالث وهو العلم بأن زيدا دخل البلد الآن. الحجة الرابعة : أن العلم بالشيء صورة مطابقة لذلك الشيء ولا شك أن حقيقة الآن غير واقع - مغايرة لحقيقة أنه واقع في الحال وحاصل، وأنه سيقع بعد ذلك - وإذا اختلفت المعلومات وجب اختلاف العلمين. الحجة الخامسة : وهو أنه يمكننا أن نعلم كونه عالما بأن الشيء الفلاني سيقع غدا حال ما نجهل كونه عالما بوقوعه حال وقوعه ولما حصل العلم بأحد هذين العلمين حال ما حصل الشك في حصول العلم الآخر علمنا تغاير العلم.

Third Proof: The Dark Room Example
Third proof: example of a person in a dark room.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Husayn's third proof that future knowledge does not automatically become present knowledge, using the example of a person in a dark room.

Core Idea
Mere persistence of a belief does not generate new knowledge; a new cognition arises from new information.

The third proof is a thought experiment: a person knows Zayd will enter tomorrow but sits in a dark room. When tomorrow comes and Zayd enters, the person still holds the original future belief but does not yet know that Zayd has entered. This shows that the future knowledge does not transform into present knowledge—a third knowledge is generated instead.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof
Read the Arabic and translation for the third proof carefully. It contains a narrative.
The example is detailed; follow the scenario step by step.
2
Understand the scenario
A person knows that Zayd will enter the city tomorrow. He then sits in a dark room, unable to perceive the outside. He retains the knowledge until the next day when Zayd enters. At that moment, does he know Zayd has entered? No, because he is in a dark room and has no new evidence.
The person's belief about the future remains, but it does not give him present knowledge.
3
Analyze the generation of new knowledge
Abu al-Husayn says that from the future knowledge plus the knowledge that tomorrow has arrived, a third knowledge arises: 'Zayd has now entered.' This new knowledge is distinct from the original future knowledge.
This shows a transformation or generation, not identity.
4
Conclude the non-identity
Since the future knowledge itself does not become present knowledge, they are not the same.
The third proof is the one Abu al-Husayn relied on most.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أبو الحسين البصري فقال: هذا المذهب باطل، ويمتنع أن يقال: بأن العلم بأن الشيء سيوجد هو نفس العلم بوجوده حال ما يصير موجوداً، وله أن يحتج على ذلك بوجوه : الحجة الأولى : أن من شرط المثلين أن يقوم كل واحد منهما مقام الآخر، والعلم بأن الشيء سيوجد لا يقوم مقام العلم بأنه موجود الآن، فإن قبل وقوع المعلوم لو اعتقدنا بأنه سيقع بعد ذلك، كان علماً، ولو اعتقدنا أنه واقع الآن كان جهلاً، وأما حال وقوعه فإنه ينقلب الأمر ، فلو اعتقدنا بأنه سيقع بعد ذلك وأنه الآن غير واقع كان جهلا ، ولو اعتقدنا أنه الآن واقع كان علمًا ، فثبت أن كل واحد منهما لا يقوم مقام الآخر، وذلك يقتضي كون هذين الاعتقادين مختلفين في الحقيقة، ومع هذا الاختلاف في الماهية والحقيقة كيف يمكن دعوى الاتحاد ؟ الحجة الثانية : أن كونه عالمًا بأنه سيقع غير مشروط بكونه واقعا في الحال، وكونه عالمًا بوقوعه مشروط بوقوعه في الحال والشيئان اللذان يكون أحدهما مشروطا بشيء والآخر لا يكون مشروطًا بذلك الشيء يمتنع أن يكون أحدهما نفس الآخر. الحجة الثالثة : وهي التي عول عليها أبو الحسين فقال : مجرد العلم بأن الشيء سيقع، لا يكون علمًا بوقوعه إذا وقع، فإن من علم أن زيدا سيدخل البلد غدًا ثم إنه جلس في بيت مظلم لا يميز فيه بين الليل والنهار وبقي مستديما لذلك العلم حتى جاء الغد ودخل زيد البلد فههنا هذا الشخص بمجرد علمه بأن زيدا سيدخل البلد غدًا لا عالمًا بأنه دخل الآن في البلد، فثبت بهذا أن العلم بأن الشيء سيوجد غدًا لا يصير يكون نفس العلم بوجوده إذا وجد. بلى من علم أن زيدا سيدخل البلد غدًا ثم علم حصول الغد فحينئذ يتولد من هذين العلمين علم ثالث وهو العلم بأن زيدا دخل البلد الآن. الحجة الرابعة : أن العلم بالشيء صورة مطابقة لذلك الشيء ولا شك أن حقيقة الآن غير واقع - مغايرة لحقيقة أنه واقع في الحال وحاصل، وأنه سيقع بعد ذلك - وإذا اختلفت المعلومات وجب اختلاف العلمين. الحجة الخامسة : وهو أنه يمكننا أن نعلم كونه عالما بأن الشيء الفلاني سيقع غدا حال ما نجهل كونه عالما بوقوعه حال وقوعه ولما حصل العلم بأحد هذين العلمين حال ما حصل الشك في حصول العلم الآخر علمنا تغاير العلم.
As for Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, he said: This view is false, and it is impossible to say that knowledge that something will exist is the same knowledge as its existence when it comes to exist. He has to argue for that in several ways: The first proof: that from the condition of two similar things is that each one takes the place of the other, and knowledge that something will exist does not take the place of knowledge that it exists now. For before the occurrence of the known, if we believed that it will occur later, it is knowledge, and if we believed that it is real now, it is ignorance. As for the time of its occurrence, the matter is reversed: if we believed that it will occur later and that it is now not real, it is ignorance, and if we believed that it is now real, it is knowledge. So it is established that neither takes the place of the other, and that necessitates that these two beliefs are different in reality. With this difference in essence and reality, how can the claim of identity be possible? The second proof: that His being knowledgeable that it will occur is not conditioned on its being real in the present, and His being knowledgeable of its occurrence is conditioned on its occurrence in the present. Two things such that one is conditioned by something and the other is not conditioned by that something are impossible to be one the same as the other. The third proof: which is the one Abu al-Husayn relied on, he said: mere knowledge that something will occur is not knowledge of its occurrence when it occurs. For if someone knows that Zayd will enter the city tomorrow, then he sits in a dark room where he cannot distinguish between night and day, and he continues to hold that knowledge until morning comes and Zayd enters the city, then here this person by mere knowledge that Zayd will enter the city tomorrow is not knowing that he has now entered the city. Thus it is established that knowledge that something will exist tomorrow does not become the same knowledge as its existence when it exists. Indeed, if someone knows that Zayd will enter the city tomorrow and then knows the arrival of tomorrow, then from these two knowledges a third knowledge is generated, which is knowledge that Zayd has now entered the city. The fourth proof: that knowledge of a thing is a form corresponding to that thing, and there is no doubt that the reality of 'now not real' is different from the reality of 'it is real in the present and actual', and that 'it will occur later'. If the known objects differ, then the two knowledges must differ. The fifth proof: that we can know that He is knowing that a certain thing will occur tomorrow while we are ignorant of His being knowing of its occurrence at the time of its occurrence, and since knowledge of one of these two knowledges occurs while doubt occurs about the other knowledge, we know the two knowledges are distinct.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage presents Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's five proofs against the majority view that future knowledge is identical to present knowledge. Each proof highlights a logical or conditional difference between the two. The first proof uses substitution: before an event, believing it will happen is knowledge; believing it has happened is ignorance. After the event, the opposite is true. The two are not interchangeable, so they are distinct. The second proof: future knowledge is not conditioned on actual occurrence; present knowledge is. Things with different conditions cannot be identical. The third proof uses an example of a person in a dark room: knowledge of future entry does not automatically give knowledge of present entry; a third knowledge is generated from the future knowledge and the knowledge that time has elapsed. The fourth proof: knowledge is a form corresponding to the known; since the known (future-as-not-yet-real vs. present-as-real) differ, knowledge differs. The fifth proof: we can know God knows a future event while not knowing whether He knows it has occurred, indicating they are distinct. Together, these proofs aim to refute the identity claim and support Abu al-Husayn's position.

Key Terms
المثلين
يقوم مقام
مشروط
صورة مطابقة
تغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if a belief persists, it automatically updates itself.
Correction: Abu al-Husayn shows that new knowledge must be generated from additional information.

Checkpoint
In the third proof, what prevents the person from knowing that Zayd has entered the city?
A) He lacks knowledge that tomorrow has arrived.
B) He did not witness the entry.
C) He forgot his original knowledge.
D) He was asleep.

Key Takeaway
Future knowledge does not turn into present knowledge; a new knowledge is generated when additional conditions are met.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you were certain something would happen, but when it actually occurred, you felt caught off guard or unprepared. How does this lesson explain that gap between expectation and reality? What does that reveal about your need for fresh knowledge from Allah in each moment?
?
The person in the dark room had no new evidence—yet he assumed his old knowledge would suffice. Where in your life are you 'sitting in a dark room', relying on outdated knowledge instead of seeking the new perception Allah provides? What would it take for you to stop assuming and start seeking?
?
Abu al-Husayn shows that the knowledge of a future event and the knowledge of its present occurrence are distinct, and a third knowledge is generated only when the time arrives. How does this reshape your understanding of certainty in your faith? Are you holding onto beliefs about Allah and the afterlife that are merely 'future knowledge' without allowing them to become living, present realities through active reflection and new understanding?

Fourth Proof: Knowledge as Corresponding Form
Fourth proof: knowledge as corresponding form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Husayn's fourth argument that since knowledge is a form corresponding to its object, differing objects entail differing knowledges.

Core Idea
The objects of future and present knowledge are different, so the knowledges must be different.

The fourth proof relies on the correspondence theory of knowledge: knowledge is a mental form that matches its object. Since the object of future knowledge (something not yet existing) differs from the object of present knowledge (something existing), the knowledges themselves must differ.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth proof
Read the Arabic and translation for the fourth proof. Notice the claim: knowledge is a form corresponding to the known.
2
Understand the correspondence principle
Knowledge is a صورة مطابقة (corresponding form). If the known objects differ, the knowledges differ.
This is an epistemological principle.
3
Identify the difference in objects
The future event as not-yet-real (غير واقع) is different from the present event as real (واقع في الحال). They are distinct realities.
Abu al-Husayn lists three: 'now not real', 'real in the present', 'will occur later'.
4
Apply the principle
Since the objects differ, the knowledges must differ. Therefore, future and present knowledge cannot be identical.
The proof is straightforward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the object is the same event, so knowledge should be the same.
Correction: The object is considered under different modes (future vs present), which are different realities.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, why must future and present knowledge differ?
A) Because one is certain and the other is uncertain.
B) Because their objects (future-as-not-real vs present-as-real) differ.
C) Because one is about God and the other about creation.
D) Because one is innate and the other acquired.

Key Takeaway
Different modes of the known object yield different knowledges.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God’s knowledge of your future sins, do you treat that knowledge as something abstract and distant, or as real and personal? How does this proof’s distinction between future and present knowledge change the way you view God’s awareness of your actions right now?
?

?
If God’s knowledge of your present repentance is a different 'form' than His knowledge of your future failings, how does that impact the urgency of turning to Him now? What would it mean for you to live as though God’s present knowledge of your good deeds is the only reality that matters?

Fifth Proof: Distinct States of Knowing
Fifth proof: distinct states of knowing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Husayn's fifth proof that we can know God's knowledge of a future event while doubting His knowledge of its present occurrence, indicating they are distinct.

Core Idea
If we can know one knowledge while being uncertain about the other, they cannot be the same.

The fifth proof is an epistemic argument: we can have knowledge that God knows a future event while simultaneously being uncertain whether God knows it as present. This shows that the two knowledges are not identical, because if they were, knowledge of one would entail knowledge of the other.

Guided Path
1
Read the fifth proof
Read the Arabic and translation for the fifth proof. It is the shortest.
2
Grasp the scenario
We can know that God knows a thing will occur tomorrow, yet we may doubt whether God knows it now (if we are unsure if it has occurred). This is possible, indicating separation.
The proof uses our own epistemic state as evidence.
3
Apply the logical conclusion
If the two knowledges were identical, knowing one would automatically give knowledge of the other. Since we can know one without the other, they are distinct.
The proof relies on the principle of indiscernibility of identicals.
4
Understand the significance
This proof is straightforward and appeals to common experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أبو الحسين البصري فقال: هذا المذهب باطل، ويمتنع أن يقال: بأن العلم بأن الشيء سيوجد هو نفس العلم بوجوده حال ما يصير موجوداً، وله أن يحتج على ذلك بوجوه : الحجة الأولى : أن من شرط المثلين أن يقوم كل واحد منهما مقام الآخر، والعلم بأن الشيء سيوجد لا يقوم مقام العلم بأنه موجود الآن، فإن قبل وقوع المعلوم لو اعتقدنا بأنه سيقع بعد ذلك، كان علماً، ولو اعتقدنا أنه واقع الآن كان جهلاً، وأما حال وقوعه فإنه ينقلب الأمر ، فلو اعتقدنا بأنه سيقع بعد ذلك وأنه الآن غير واقع كان جهلا ، ولو اعتقدنا أنه الآن واقع كان علمًا ، فثبت أن كل واحد منهما لا يقوم مقام الآخر، وذلك يقتضي كون هذين الاعتقادين مختلفين في الحقيقة، ومع هذا الاختلاف في الماهية والحقيقة كيف يمكن دعوى الاتحاد ؟ الحجة الثانية : أن كونه عالمًا بأنه سيقع غير مشروط بكونه واقعا في الحال، وكونه عالمًا بوقوعه مشروط بوقوعه في الحال والشيئان اللذان يكون أحدهما مشروطا بشيء والآخر لا يكون مشروطًا بذلك الشيء يمتنع أن يكون أحدهما نفس الآخر. الحجة الثالثة : وهي التي عول عليها أبو الحسين فقال : مجرد العلم بأن الشيء سيقع، لا يكون علمًا بوقوعه إذا وقع، فإن من علم أن زيدا سيدخل البلد غدًا ثم إنه جلس في بيت مظلم لا يميز فيه بين الليل والنهار وبقي مستديما لذلك العلم حتى جاء الغد ودخل زيد البلد فههنا هذا الشخص بمجرد علمه بأن زيدا سيدخل البلد غدًا لا عالمًا بأنه دخل الآن في البلد، فثبت بهذا أن العلم بأن الشيء سيوجد غدًا لا يصير يكون نفس العلم بوجوده إذا وجد. بلى من علم أن زيدا سيدخل البلد غدًا ثم علم حصول الغد فحينئذ يتولد من هذين العلمين علم ثالث وهو العلم بأن زيدا دخل البلد الآن. الحجة الرابعة : أن العلم بالشيء صورة مطابقة لذلك الشيء ولا شك أن حقيقة الآن غير واقع - مغايرة لحقيقة أنه واقع في الحال وحاصل، وأنه سيقع بعد ذلك - وإذا اختلفت المعلومات وجب اختلاف العلمين. الحجة الخامسة : وهو أنه يمكننا أن نعلم كونه عالما بأن الشيء الفلاني سيقع غدا حال ما نجهل كونه عالما بوقوعه حال وقوعه ولما حصل العلم بأحد هذين العلمين حال ما حصل الشك في حصول العلم الآخر علمنا تغاير العلم.

Commentary
This passage presents Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's five proofs against the majority view that future knowledge is identical to present knowledge. Each proof highlights a logical or conditional difference between the two. The first proof uses substitution: before an event, believing it will happen is knowledge; believing it has happened is ignorance. After the event, the opposite is true. The two are not interchangeable, so they are distinct. The second proof: future knowledge is not conditioned on actual occurrence; present knowledge is. Things with different conditions cannot be identical. The third proof uses an example of a person in a dark room: knowledge of future entry does not automatically give knowledge of present entry; a third knowledge is generated from the future knowledge and the knowledge that time has elapsed. The fourth proof: knowledge is a form corresponding to the known; since the known (future-as-not-yet-real vs. present-as-real) differ, knowledge differs. The fifth proof: we can know God knows a future event while not knowing whether He knows it has occurred, indicating they are distinct. Together, these proofs aim to refute the identity claim and support Abu al-Husayn's position.

Key Terms
المثلين
يقوم مقام
مشروط
صورة مطابقة
تغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key observation in the fifth proof?
A) We always know God's knowledge fully.
B) We can know God knows a future event while being unsure if He knows it as present.
C) God's knowledge is identical to His essence.
D) Future knowledge is impossible for humans.

Key Takeaway
Epistemic asymmetry reveals the distinctness of future and present divine knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
This proof relies on noticing a difference in my own certainty. Where in my faith am I trying to claim a certainty I do not actually have? Will I confess that doubt to God and ask Him to increase my knowledge as He wills, rather than pretending to have clarity?

Abu al-Husayn's Theory of Change in God's Knowledge
Razi presents Abu al-Husayn's view that God's knowledge of changing particulars itself changes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's argument that God's knowledge of changing particulars changes because the presence of the object in its current state is a condition for the causal efficacy of the divine essence.

Core Idea
God's knowledge changes because the known object changes, and the condition for causing that knowledge vanishes.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents the distinctive theory of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, who argued that God's knowledge of temporal particulars is not static but changes as the particulars themselves change. This view directly challenges the classical Ash'arī position that God's knowledge is eternal and unchanging. Let's examine the logical steps of his argument and the two objections that arise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal English translation. Pay attention to the key terms: al-mūjib (the cause), sharṭ al-ījāb (condition of causation), and ḥuḍūr al-ma‘lūmāt (presence of known things).
2
Identify cause and condition
Notice that Abu al-Husayn identifies God's essence as the cause of His knowledge, but adds that the presence of the known object in a specific state is a condition for that causation to be effective.
This is a conditional causal relationship.
3
Trace the logical sequence
Follow the argument: when the object occurs in a certain way, the condition is fulfilled, so knowledge of that occurrence arises. When the object changes, the condition ceases, so that knowledge ceases and new knowledge arises.
This sequence implies change in God's knowledge over time.
4
Contrast with the masters' view
Reflect on how this differs from the classical Ash'arī view, which holds that God's knowledge is eternal and unchanging. Abu al-Husayn's theory is an attempt to reconcile God's omniscience with the changing nature of temporal particulars.
The passage says he invalidated the masters' statement with proofs.
5
Note the objections
The passage concludes by stating that two difficult questions arise against this theory. This sets up the next part of the discussion, which will examine those objections.
We will explore those objections in the following lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن أبا الحسين البصري كما أبطل بهذه الدلائل قول المشايخ التزم وقوع التغير في علم الله تعالى بالجزئيات المتغيرة فقال: الموجب لكونه تعالى عالما بالمعلومات هو ذاته، لكن شرط هذا الإيجاب حضور تلك المعلومات، فإذا حصل المعلوم واقعا على وجه معين حصل شرط كون الذات موجبة للعلم بوقوع ذلك الشيء على ذلك الوجه، فيحصل ذلك العلم، وإذا علم وقوع ذلك المعلوم على هذا الوجه زال شرط الإيجاب ولا جرم يزول ذلك العلم ويحدث علم آخر بوقوع ذلك المعلوم على الوجه الثاني. فهذا مذهبه في هذا الباب إلا أنه يتوجه عليه سؤالان صعبان:
And know that Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, just as he invalidated with these proofs the statement of the masters, committed himself to the occurrence of change in God's knowledge of changing particulars. He said: The cause that makes Him, exalted, knowledgeable of known things is His essence, but the condition for this causation is the presence of those known things. So when the known occurs in a particular manner, the condition for the essence to cause knowledge of the occurrence of that thing in that manner is fulfilled, so that knowledge occurs. And when the occurrence of that known in that manner ceases, the condition for causation ceases, and inevitably that knowledge ceases, and another knowledge occurs of the occurrence of that known in the second manner. This is his doctrine in this chapter, except that two difficult questions arise against it:
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage presents the doctrine of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri regarding God's knowledge of changing particulars. He argued that God's essence is the cause of His knowledge, but this causation is conditional upon the presence of the known objects as they occur. When a particular event occurs in a specific way, God's essence produces knowledge of it in that way. When the event changes, the condition for that knowledge ceases, so that knowledge ceases and a new knowledge arises corresponding to the new state. Thus God's knowledge changes along with the changing particulars. The author then notes that two difficult objections face this theory, which will be presented next.

Correction: Abu al-Husayn's theory does not deny God's knowledge; rather it affirms that God knows each particular as it actually occurs, which requires that His knowledge be dynamic.

Abu al-Husayn argues that God's knowledge of changing particulars is itself changing, contingent upon the condition of the object's presence in that state.

First Objection: Eternity or Temporality of God's Knowledge
Razi's first objection against Abu al-Husayn's view on divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first logical objection demonstrating that every possible status of God's past knowledge leads to an impossible consequence.

Core Idea
Every option for God's past knowledge leads to an absurdity.

This lesson presents Razi's first objection against Abu al-Husayn's view that God's knowledge changes. The objection systematically tests each possible status of the knowledge God had before creation, showing that each leads to a contradiction that undermines key theological proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire objection
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Let the logical flow sink in before dissecting it.
The passage is a single continuous argument with branching conditionals.
2
Identify the initial dilemma
The objection starts with God's knowledge before creation: after creation, does that knowledge persist or cease? Understand why persistence is impossible (knowing 'will create' for an already created thing).
If it persists, contradiction directly.
3
Trace the cessation branch
If the knowledge ceases, then it must have been either eternal or temporal. Examine each: if eternal, its cessation permits the non-existence of an eternal, undermining the proof of creation. If temporal, then it must be part of an infinite series or have a first member.
Each sub-branch also leads to a problem.
4
Examine the temporal sub-branches
If the temporal knowledge is preceded by an infinite regress of temporal knowledges, that entails an infinite regress of events, which also undermines the creation proof. If it terminates at a first temporal knowledge, then God was ignorant in pre-eternity, which is impossible.
All paths end in contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الأول: أنه تعالى قبل أن خلق العالم كان عالما بأنه سيخلقه، فإذا خلق العالم فهل زال العلم الأول أو لم يزل؟ فإن لم يزل كان عالما بأنه سيخلقه الآن مع أنه في نفسه مخلوق، وذلك محال. وأما إن زال العلم الأول فذلك العلم الذي زال كان قديمًا أو حادثًا؟ فإن كان قديمًا كان هذا قولا بجواز عدم القديم، وحينئذ يبطل دليله على حدوث الأجسام؛ لأن مبنى ذلك على أن عدم القديم لا يجوز، وأما إن كان ذلك العلم محدثًا فهذا العلم المحدث هل كان مسبوقا بعلم آخر لا إلى أول، أو لم يكن كذلك؟ فإن كان الأول كان هذا قولا بحوادث لا أول لها، وهذا يبطل عليه دليل حدوث الأجسام، وأما إن انتهت هذه العلوم إلى علم محدث غير مسبوق بعلم آخر كان هذا قولاً بأنه تعالى ما كان في الأزل عالما بأحوال هذه التغيرات، فيكون هذا جهلا مطلقًا لله تعالى، وذلك باطل قطعا.
The First Question: That God, exalted, before He created the world, was knowledgeable that He would create it. So when He created the world, has the first knowledge ceased or not? If it has not ceased, then He would be knowledgeable that He will create it now while it is in itself created, and that is impossible. But if the first knowledge has ceased, then that knowledge which ceased—was it eternal or temporal? If it was eternal, then this is a statement permitting the non-existence of the eternal, and thereby his proof of the creation of bodies is invalidated, because its basis is that the non-existence of the eternal is not permissible. And if that knowledge was temporal, then this temporal knowledge—was it preceded by another knowledge without beginning, or not? If the first, then this is a statement of events without beginning, and this invalidates his proof of the creation of bodies. But if these knowledges terminate at a temporal knowledge not preceded by another knowledge, then this is a statement that God, exalted, was not in pre-eternity knowledgeable of the states of these changes, and that would be absolute ignorance of God, exalted, and that is definitively false.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two difficult objections against Abu al-Husayn's theory of changing divine knowledge. The objection considers God's knowledge before creation: He knew He would create the world. After creation, did that knowledge cease? If not, then God would still know that He will create the world now, which is false because it is already created. If the knowledge did cease, then was that knowledge eternal or temporal? If eternal, then it would entail that an eternal thing can cease to exist, which undermines the proof for the creation of bodies (which relies on the impossibility of non-existence of the eternal). If it was temporal, then was it preceded by an infinite series of temporal knowledges? If so, that would entail an infinite regress of temporal events, again undermining the proof for creation. If the series terminates at a first temporal knowledge without a predecessor, then God would not have known the changing states from eternity, which implies ignorance—impossible for God. Thus every option leads to a contradiction.

Key Terms
قديم
حادث
عدم القديم
حوادث لا أول لها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Abu al-Husayn can simply say God's knowledge is temporal but with a beginning, avoiding infinite regress.
Correction: The objection shows that if temporal knowledge has a beginning, then before that beginning God was ignorant of changing states, which is impossible for God.

Checkpoint
What is one consequence of saying that the knowledge which ceased was eternal?
A) God would be ignorant.
B) It would permit the non-existence of an eternal thing, undermining the proof of creation.
C) It would prove the world is eternal.
D) It would mean God's knowledge is unchanging.

Key Takeaway
Razi's first objection demonstrates that any account of God's past knowledge leads to a contradiction, thereby challenging the coherence of Abu al-Husayn's view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This lesson forces you to see that any attempt to temporalize God's knowledge leads to absurdity. ?
Razi's objection shows that any attempt to limit God's knowledge to temporal terms ends in ignorance. Is there an area of your life where you have limited God's knowledge or power in your mind? How will you repent from such thoughts and strive to affirm God's absolute perfection in your heart?

The Philosophers' Proof Against Change in God
Razi enumerates the philosophers' tripartite proof against change in God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' tripartite proof that God's attributes cannot change, and why dependency on an external factor is incompatible with divine self-sufficiency.

Core Idea
God's attributes are either always present, always absent, or dependent on an external factor—but dependence is impossible.

This lesson examines the second objection in Razi's discussion: the philosophers' reasoned argument that change in God's essence or attributes is impossible. They base their proof on a simple logical division concerning whether God's essence alone is sufficient for an attribute's existence or non-existence. Understanding this proof is crucial for grasping the Islamic theological commitment to divine immutability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage aloud
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the logical structure: three distinct cases are presented.
The text cuts off mid-sentence; focus on the clear cases given.
2
Identify the three cases
The proof divides into three possibilities: God's essence is sufficient for the attribute's existence, sufficient for its non-existence, or sufficient for neither. Locate each in the text.
3
Understand the consequences
For the first two cases, the attribute must be eternal (either always present or always absent). For the third case, the attribute depends on an external factor.
The third case is problematic because God cannot depend on anything external.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Even though the passage is incomplete, consider why the philosophers consider change impossible. How does this proof relate to God's self-sufficiency?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني: وهو أن الفلاسفة أقاموا البرهان المطلق على امتناع وقوع التغيرات في ذات الله تعالى وفي صفاته فقالوا كل صفة يفرض ثبوتها فذات الله تعالى من حيث هي هي إما أن تكون كافية في ثبوتها أو كافية في انتفائها، أو لا تكون كافية لا في ثبوتها ولا في انتفائها، فإن كانت ذاته سبحانه كافية في ثبوتها وجب ثبوتها للذات وأبدا حتى تكون تلك الصفة دائمة الثبوت بدوام ذاته، وإن كانت ذاته سبحانه وتعالى كافية في انتفائها وجب انتفاؤها عن الذات أولا وأبدا حتى تكون دائمة الانتفاء بدوام ذاته، وأما القسم الثالث، وهو أن يقال: إن ذاته سبحانه غير كافية في ثبوت تلك الصفة ولا في انتفائها فعلى هذا التقدير يكون ثبوت تلك الصفة وعدمها موقوفين على ثبوت شيء منفصل وعلى عدمه. فنقول: ذات الله تعالى لا تنفك عن ثبوت هذه الصفة وعن عدمها، وثبوت هذه الصفة وعدمها موقوفان على ثبوت ذلك الشيء المنفصل وعدمه، والموقوف على
The Second Question: That the philosophers have established the absolute proof of the impossibility of changes occurring in the essence of God, exalted, and in His attributes. They said: Every attribute whose existence is supposed: the essence of God, exalted, in itself is either sufficient for its existence or sufficient for its non-existence, or not sufficient for either its existence or its non-existence. If His essence, glorified, is sufficient for its existence, then its existence for the essence is necessary forever, so that that attribute is always existent with the duration of His essence. And if His essence, glorified and exalted, is sufficient for its non-existence, then its non-existence from the essence is necessary from the first and forever, so that it is always non-existent with the duration of His essence. As for the third division, namely that one says: His essence, glorified, is not sufficient for the existence of that attribute nor for its non-existence, then on this assumption the existence of that attribute and its non-existence are dependent on the existence of something separate and on its non-existence. We say: The essence of God, exalted, is not free from the existence of this attribute nor from its non-existence, and the existence of this attribute and its non-existence are dependent on the existence of that separate thing and its non-existence, and the dependent on...
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection, which is the philosophers' absolute proof that change cannot occur in God's essence or attributes. The proof is a tripartite division: for any attribute, God's essence is either sufficient for its existence, sufficient for its non-existence, or neither. If sufficient for existence, the attribute must always exist; if sufficient for non-existence, it must always be absent; if sufficient for neither, then the attribute's existence or non-existence depends on an external factor. Since God's essence is necessary and self-sufficient, dependency on an external factor would contradict His nature. The text cuts off mid-sentence while stating that God's essence is never free from either having or lacking the attribute, and that it depends on that separate thing. The argument is incomplete in the provided window, but the direction is clear: the philosophers aim to prove that any change in God's attributes would require an external cause, which is impossible.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' proof denies that God has any attributes at all.
Correction: The proof does not deny attributes; it argues that any attribute of God must be eternal and unchanging, because change would require an external cause, which contradicts divine self-sufficiency.

The Dilemma of God's Knowledge of Particulars
Razi presents a dilemma on God's prior knowledge of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma presented by Imam Razi regarding whether God's prior knowledge of creating the world remains after creation and why all options lead to absurdities.

Core Idea
God's knowledge cannot change without leading to contradictions.

Imam Razi presents a powerful objection against the idea that God's knowledge changes with changing events. This objection takes the form of a dilemma, where each possible option leads to an absurdity. The argument challenges the opponent to either accept that God's knowledge remains unchanged, which contradicts the state of creation, or that it changes, which leads to deeper theological problems.

Guided Path
1
Understand the starting premise
Begin by recognizing that before creation, God knew He would create the world. This is the initial assumption from which the dilemma unfolds.
2
First branch: knowledge remains
If God's knowledge that He will create remains after creation, then He knows something that is already existent as future, which is contradictory.
This branch leads to impossibility directly.
3
Second branch: knowledge ceases
If the knowledge ceases, then we must examine the nature of that ceased knowledge: was it eternal or originated?
Each option leads to a further dilemma.
4
Sub-branches of the ceased knowledge
If eternal, then the eternal can cease, undermining proofs for the temporal origination of bodies. If originated, then we ask if it was preceded by an infinite chain of knowledges or not.
5
Conclude the impossibility
All branches end in absurdity: either contradiction, permitting the non-existence of the eternal, infinite regress, or God being ignorant. Therefore, the original position that God's knowledge changes is untenable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الأول : أنه تعالى قبل أن خلق العالم كان عالما بأنه سيخلقه، فإذا خلق العالم فهل زال العلم الأول أو لم يزل ؟ فإن لم يزل كان عالما بأنه سيخلقه الآن مع أنه في نفسه مخلوق، وذلك محال وأما إن زال العلم الأول فذلك العلم الذي زال كان قديمًا أو حادثًا ؟ فإن كان قديمًا كان هذا قولا بجواز عدم القديم، وحينئذ يبطل دليله على حدوث الأجسام؛ لأن مبنى ذلك على أن عدم القديم لا يجوز، وأما إن كان ذلك العلم محدثًا فهذا العلم المحدث هل كان مسبوقا بعلم آخر لا إلى أول، أو لم يكن كذلك ؟ فإن كان الأول كان هذا قولا بحوادث لا أول لها، وهذا يبطل عليه دليل حدوث الأجسام، وأما إن انتهت هذه العلوم إلى علم محدث غير مسبوق بعلم آخر كان هذا قولاً بأنه تعالى ما كان في الأزل عالما بأحوال هذه التغيرات، فيكون هذا جهلا مطلقًا لله تعالى، وذلك باطل قطعا.
The first question: that He, exalted, before He created the world was knowing that He would create it. So when He created the world, does the first knowledge cease or not? If it does not cease, then He would be knowing that He will create it now while it is in itself created—and that is impossible. But if the first knowledge ceases, then that knowledge which ceased—was it eternal or originated? If it was eternal, then this is a statement permitting the non-existence of the eternal, and thereby his proof for the temporal origination of bodies is invalidated, because its basis is that the non-existence of the eternal is not permissible. But if that knowledge was originated, then was this originated knowledge preceded by another knowledge without beginning, or not? If the former, then this is a statement of originated events without beginning, and this invalidates his proof for the temporal origination of bodies. But if these knowledges terminate at an originated knowledge not preceded by another knowledge, then this is a statement that He, exalted, was not in pre-eternity knowing the states of these changes, and this would be absolute ignorance of God, exalted—and that is definitively false.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents a dilemma (the first objection) against a position (likely that of those who claim God's knowledge changes with changing particulars). The objection proceeds by considering two scenarios after creation: either God's prior knowledge that He would create the world remains, or it ceases. If it remains, then God would simultaneously know that He will create the world while it is already created—a contradiction. If it ceases, then we ask whether that ceased knowledge was eternal or originated. If eternal, then this allows for the annihilation of the eternal, which undermines the proof for the temporal origination of bodies (since that proof relies on the impossibility of the eternal ceasing to exist). If the ceased knowledge was originated, then we ask whether that originated knowledge was preceded by another knowledge without beginning (infinite regress) or not. An infinite regress of originated knowledge implies events without beginning, again undermining the proof for temporal origination. If the regress ends at an originated knowledge not preceded by any knowledge, then God would have been ignorant of those changes before that first originated knowledge—which is absolute ignorance, impossible for God. Thus all branches lead to an absurdity.

Key Terms
علم
جزئيات
قديم
حادث
حوادث لا أول لها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God's knowledge changes, it is simply a new act of knowing; but the objection shows the logical problems with the nature of that change.
Correction: The dilemma demonstrates that any change implies either a contradiction, the possibility of the eternal ceasing, an infinite regress, or pre-eternal ignorance, all of which are theologically untenable.

Checkpoint
According to the first objection, if God's prior knowledge of creating the world ceases and that knowledge was eternal, what consequence follows?
A) It confirms the proof for the temporal origination of bodies.
B) It permits the non-existence of the eternal, thus invalidating the proof for the temporal origination of bodies.
C) It implies an infinite regress of knowledges.
D) It leads to God being ignorant in pre-eternity.

Key Takeaway
The objection shows that any change in God's knowledge leads to absurdities, thus defending the doctrine of God's omniscience and immutability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge of your every action is eternal and unchanging, how does that affect the way you approach repentance? Do you truly believe that your turning back to Him was already known before you were born, and yet He still calls you? What does that mean for the sincerity of your return?
?
This dilemma forces us to see that God cannot learn or forget. When you feel that God is distant or unaware of your struggles, does this truth—that He has always known your pain without any change—bring you comfort or discomfort? Why?
?
Ponder the absurdity of God's ignorance. If even one moment of ignorance were possible for God, the entire structure of tawheed collapses. Do you live with a deep, lived conviction that every detail of your life is encompassed by His eternal knowledge, or do you sometimes act as if He does not see? What specific area of your life would change if you truly internalized this?

The Philosophers' Absolute Proof Against Change in God's Attributes
Razi presents the philosophers' absolute proof against change in God's attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' categorical proof that change in God's attributes is impossible, based on a tripartite division regarding the sufficiency of God's essence.

Core Idea
The philosophers prove that any change in God's attributes leads to a contradiction with God's necessary existence.

This passage presents the philosophers' rigorous argument against attributing change to God. They start by dividing every possible attribute into three cases based on whether God's essence is sufficient for its existence, its non-existence, or neither. From this exhaustive division, they derive that change is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Get a feel for the logical flow.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the tripartite division
The proof divides all attributes into three categories: where God's essence is sufficient for the attribute's existence, sufficient for its absence, or neither. Identify these three cases.
3
Trace the third case to the contradiction
Focus on the third case where God's essence is not sufficient. Follow how this leads to dependence on an external factor, which implies contingency, contradicting God's necessary existence.
This is the key move of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني: وهو أن الفلاسفة أقاموا البرهان المطلق على امتناع وقوع التغيرات في ذات الله تعالى وفي صفاته فقالوا كل صفة يفرض ثبوتها فذات الله تعالى من حيث هي هي إما أن تكون كافية في ثبوتها أو كافية في انتفائها، أو لا تكون كافية لا في ثبوتها ولا في انتفائها، فإن كانت ذاته سبحانه كافية في ثبوتها وجب ثبوتها للذات أولا وأبدا حتى تكون تلك الصفة دائمة الثبوت بدوام ذاته، وإن كانت ذاته سبحانه وتعالى كافية في انتفائها وجب انتفاؤها عن الذات أولا وأبدا حتى تكون دائمة الانتفاء بدوام ذاته، وأما القسم الثالث، وهو أن يقال: إن ذاته سبحانه غير كافية في ثبوت تلك الصفة ولا في انتفائها فعلى هذا التقدير يكون ثبوت تلك الصفة وعدمها موقوفين على ثبوت شيء منفصل وعلى عدمه. فنقول : ذات الله تعالى لا تنفك عن ثبوت هذه الصفة وعن عدمها، وثبوت هذه الصفة وعدمها موقوفان على ثبوت ذلك الشيء المنفصل وعدمه، والموقوف على الغير موقوف على الغير، فذات الله تعالى مفتقرة في تحققها إلى الغير، والمفتقر في تحققه إلى الغير ممكن لذاته، فيلزم أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته ممكن الوجود لذاته، وذلك محال فثبت أن التغير في صفات الله تعالى محال.
The second question: that the philosophers established the absolute proof of the impossibility of occurrences of changes in the essence of God, exalted, and in His attributes. They said: every attribute whose existence is supposed—the essence of God, exalted, considered solely in itself—either is sufficient for its existence, or sufficient for its non-existence, or is not sufficient either for its existence or for its non-existence. If His essence, glorified, is sufficient for its existence, then its existence for the essence is necessary from the first and always, so that attribute is permanent in existence by the permanence of His essence. If His essence, glorified and exalted, is sufficient for its non-existence, then its non-existence from the essence is necessary from the first and always, so that it is permanent in non-existence by the permanence of His essence. As for the third division—that it is said: His essence, glorified, is not sufficient for the existence of that attribute nor for its non-existence—then on this supposition, the existence of that attribute and its non-existence are dependent on the existence of something separate and on its non-existence. We say: The essence of God, exalted, is not free from the existence of this attribute nor from its non-existence, and the existence of this attribute and its non-existence are dependent on the existence of that separate thing and its non-existence. That which depends on another depends on another; thus the essence of God, exalted, is in need of another for its actualization, and that which in its actualization is in need of another is possible in itself. Hence it would follow that the Necessary Existence in Himself is possible existence in himself—and that is impossible. So it is established that change in the attributes of God, exalted, is impossible.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' categorical proof against change in God's attributes. The proof works by a tripartite division regarding any supposed attribute: either God's essence is sufficient for its existence, sufficient for its non-existence, or neither. In the first two cases, the attribute is permanent (either always existent or always non-existent), so no change occurs. In the third case, the attribute's existence or non-existence depends on an external factor. Since God's essence cannot be without either the attribute's existence or non-existence, and both depend on an external factor, God's essence becomes dependent on that factor for its actuality (i.e., for being qualified). Anything dependent on another is possible in itself. Therefore the Necessary Existence would be possible in itself—a contradiction. Hence change is impossible. The proof is categorical, covering all possibilities.

Key Terms
برهان مطلق
ذات
صفة
كافية
موقوف على الغير
ممكن لذاته
واجب الوجود لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof only applies to essential attributes, not to all attributes.
Correction: The tripartite division is exhaustive for any supposed attribute, so the proof covers all attributes, including actions.

Checkpoint
In the philosophers' proof, what follows if God's essence is not sufficient for the existence or non-existence of an attribute?
A) The attribute is impossible.
B) The attribute is necessary for the essence.
C) God's essence depends on an external factor, leading to contingency.
D) The attribute changes perpetually.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that any change in God's attributes would imply dependence and contingency, which is impossible for the Necessary Existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial, do you find yourself wishing that God's attribute of mercy would 'change' to give you relief on your timeline? How does this proof challenge that expectation?
?
If God's attributes are eternally and unchangeably perfect, how does that confront your desire for God to act in a way that feels new or different from how He has always been?
?
Does your heart truly rest in the constancy of God's attributes, or do you secretly prefer a God who adapts to your changing desires? What would it mean for you to submit to His unchangeable perfection?

Restricting the Proof to Essential Attributes
The second group responds by distinguishing essential from relational attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second group argues that the philosophers' proof against change applies only to essential attributes, not to relational attributes, and what this implies for the possibility of change in God's relational attributes.

Core Idea
Change in God's relational attributes is possible because they depend on external things.

The philosophers argued that change in any divine attribute implies imperfection. But the second group in this debate distinguishes between two types of attributes: those that are essential to God's being and those that are relational. In this passage, they argue that the philosophers' proof only blocks change in the essential attributes, leaving relational attributes open to change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Try to grasp the overall argument before diving into details.
2
Identify the two types of attributes
Notice the distinction between essential attributes (الصفات الحقيقية) and relational attributes (الصفات الإضافية). The proof applies only to the former.
3
Examine the example of 'withness'
The group gives an example: when a thing comes into existence, God is 'with' it; when it perishes, that relation ends. This shows change in a relational attribute.
The Arabic term for 'withness' is 'المعية'.
4
Understand the generalization
From the example, they generalize that all attachments (التعلقات) of God's attributes to created things are relations and additions, not essential attributes.
5
Reflect on the implication
This response limits the scope of the philosophers' categorical proof. It suggests that change in relational attributes does not imply imperfection in God's essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللفريق الثاني أن يقولوا : ما ذكرتم من الدليل المانع من التغير إنما يجري في الصفات الحقيقية، أما الصفات الإضافية فلا يمكن منع التغير فيها، وكيف لا نقول هذا، وإذا وجد حادث فإن الله تعالى يكون معه فإذا فني ذلك الحادث بطلت تلك المعية ؟ وهذا يقتضي وقوع التغير في الإضافات، وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: هذه التعلقات من باب النسب والإضافات، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لم يمتنع وقوع التغيرات فيها.
And for the second group, they may say: What you have mentioned of the proof preventing change applies only to essential attributes. As for relational attributes (al-sifat al-idafiyya), it is not possible to prevent change in them. And how could we not say this? When an originated thing exists, then God, exalted, is 'with' it; when that originated thing perishes, that 'withness' (al-ma'iyya) ceases. This necessitates the occurrence of change in relations. And if this is established, we say: these attachments (al-ta'allaqat) are of the category of relations and additions. And if the matter is so, then occurrence of changes in them is not impossible.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage records a response from the 'second group' (apparently those who allow change in God's knowledge). They argue that the philosophers' proof (from the previous lesson) only applies to essential attributes (sifat haqiqiyya)—attributes that inhere in the essence—not to relational attributes (sifat idafiyya) that are grounded in external relations. As an example, when something new comes into existence, God's attribute of 'being with' that thing (al-ma'iyya) arises; when that thing ceases, the relation ceases. This is a clear instance of change in a relational attribute. The group then generalizes: all 'attachments' (ta'allaqat) of God's attributes to created things are relations and additions, and thus change in them is not impossible. This response seeks to defuse the philosophers' categorical proof by restricting its scope.

Key Terms
الصفات الحقيقية
الصفات الإضافية
المعية
التعلقات
النسب والإضافات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all of God's attributes are essential and thus unchangeable.
Correction: The passage distinguishes between essential attributes (intrinsic) and relational attributes (dependent on external things). Change is possible in the latter.

Checkpoint
According to the second group, why is change possible in relational attributes?
A) Because they are not real attributes.
B) Because they are not part of God's essence.
C) Because they depend on external things that come and go.
D) Because God's essence changes with them.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' proof against divine change only applies to essential attributes, not to relational attributes, which depend on external things and can change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Are you comfortable with the idea that God's relation to you is real and responsive, not just a static decree? How does this truth press you to examine the reality of your connection to Him—is it alive or merely assumed?

Hisham's First Proof: The Necessitation of All Acts
Hisham states the premise and deduces that all acts are either necessary or impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Hisham ibn al-Hakam argues from God's eternal foreknowledge to the conclusion that all human acts are either necessary or impossible.

Core Idea
Eternal foreknowledge makes all acts inevitable — there is no middle ground.

Hisham ibn al-Hakam challenges the mainstream doctrine of divine foreknowledge by drawing a logical consequence: if God knows all particulars eternally, then whatever He knows to occur is necessary, and whatever He knows not to occur is impossible. In this lesson, we examine the first step of his proof, which reduces all actions to these two categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the premise
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice that Hisham starts with a conditional: if God knows all particulars eternally, then He knows all human acts.
The premise is that God knows everything that will and will not happen.
2
Understand the necessitation rule
Hisham applies a key principle: whatever God knows to occur is necessary (wajib al-wuqu'), and whatever He knows not to occur is impossible (mumtani' al-wuqu').
This rule is crucial for the argument.
3
Recognize the disjunctive conclusion
From the rule, it follows that all acts of creatures are either necessary or impossible. There is no third option.
This sets up the next lesson's consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج هشام بن الحكم على هذا المذهب بوجوه : الأول : لو كان تعالى عالمًا في الأزل بجميع الجزئيات التي توجد في لا يزال لكان عالما بكل ما يصدر من الناس من أفعالهم وعالما بما لا يصدر عنهم، وكل ما علم الله تعالى وقوعه كان واجب الوقوع، وكل ما علم الله تعالى عدم وقوعه كان ممتنع الوقوع فيلزم أن يقال: جميع أفعال الخلق إما واجبة الوقوع، أو ممتنعة الوقوع، ولو كان الأمر كذلك لم يكن لشيء من الحيوانات قدرة على الفعل، لأن الذي كان معلوم الله تعالى أنه يوجد يكون واجب الوقوع، والذي علم أنه لا يصدر منه يكون ممتنع الوقوع ، ولا قدرة البتة لا على ما يكون واجب الوقوع، ولا على ما يكون ممتنع الوقوع، وهذا يقتضي أن لا يكون لله تعالى قدرة البتة، وأن لا يكون لشيء من المخلوقات قدرة البتة وأن تكون التكاليف وبعثة الرسل كلها عبئًا ضائعا، وأن يكون الوعد والوعيد والثواب والعقاب كلها عبثًا وجوراً، وهذا يبطل القول بالربوبية، لأن نفي القدرة عن الله تعالى يبطل القول بالربوبية، ويبطل القول أيضًا بالعبودية ؛ لأن العبد إذا لم تكن له قدرة على العبودية، كان الأمر والنهي عبثاً، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك
And Hisham ibn al-Hakam argued for this doctrine with several proofs. The first: If He, exalted, were in pre-eternity knowing all particulars that come to be in the future, then He would be knowing everything that issues from people of their actions and knowing what does not issue from them. And everything that God, exalted, knows to occur is necessarily occurring, and everything that God, exalted, knows not to occur is impossible to occur. Consequently, it would be said: all acts of creatures are either necessarily occurring or impossible to occur. And if the matter were so, no animal would have any power over action, because what God knew to exist is necessarily occurring, and what He knew not to issue from him is impossible to occur. And there is absolutely no power over what is necessarily occurring nor over what is impossible to occur. This necessitates that God, exalted, has absolutely no power, and that no creature has any power at all, and that the obligations and the sending of messengers are all a wasted burden, and that the promise and threat, reward and punishment, are all futile and unjust. This invalidates the affirmation of Lordship (al-rububiyya), because negating power from God, exalted, invalidates the affirmation of Lordship, and also invalidates the affirmation of servitude (al-'ubudiyya), because if the servant has no power over servitude, then command and prohibition are futile. And if the matter is so...
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof of Hisham ibn al-Hakam against the view that God has eternal knowledge of all particulars (the position of the majority). Hisham argues that if God knows all acts eternally, then what He knows will happen must happen (necessary) and what He knows will not happen cannot happen (impossible). Thus all acts become either necessary or impossible. In neither case can any agent have power (qudra) over the act, since one cannot prevent the necessary nor bring about the impossible. Consequently, God would have no power (since nothing can be otherwise), and creatures would have no power. If so, religious obligations (takalif), sending prophets, promises, threats, reward and punishment become meaningless—an unjust waste. This undermines the very concept of Lordship (since Lordship implies power) and servitude (since the servant must have power to obey). The proof is a reductio ad absurdum of the majority position.

Key Terms
الأزل
الجزئيات
واجب الوقوع
ممتنع الوقوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think foreknowledge does not affect the nature of the act.
Correction: Hisham argues that infallible foreknowledge makes the outcome fixed—so the act becomes necessary or impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Hisham's first proof, what follows from God's eternal knowledge of all particulars?
A) All acts are avoided by humans.
B) All acts are either necessary or impossible.
C) All acts are impossible.
D) All acts are possible.

Key Takeaway
Hisham reduces all human acts to two modal categories based on divine foreknowledge: necessary or impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every action you have ever done or will do was either necessary or impossible in God's eternal knowledge, how does that change the way you view your past mistakes? Are you willing to accept that they were inevitable, or does that thought unsettle your sense of personal accountability?
?
When you make plans for the future, do you truly believe that the outcome is already determined in God's knowledge? How does that conviction affect the intensity of your reliance on Him versus your own efforts?
?
Hisham's argument leaves no room for 'maybe' in human acts. Are you comfortable living without the illusion of contingency, or do you find yourself resisting this conclusion? What does your resistance reveal about your attachment to your own free will?

Hisham's First Proof: The Negation of Power and the Futility of Obligations
Hisham deduces the negation of power and the futility of all religious concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Hisham ibn al-Hakam concludes that divine foreknowledge renders all power—divine and human—impossible, and thereby invalidates religious obligations and the concepts of Lordship and servitude.

Core Idea
If all acts are fixed, no one has power, and religion becomes meaningless.

Having established that all acts are either necessary or impossible, Hisham now draws devastating consequences. He argues that no agent—neither God nor creatures—can have power over such acts, because power requires the possibility of doing or not doing. This eliminates the basis for religious obligations, prophethood, reward and punishment, and ultimately undermines the very concepts of Lordship and servitude.

Guided Path
1
Understand the consequence for power
If an act is necessary, you cannot prevent it; if impossible, you cannot bring it about. Thus no creature has power over any act.
2
Realize the implications for God's power
Hisham argues that God also loses power, because His acts are similarly determined by His own knowledge.
This is a key point: divine foreknowledge would also constrain God.
3
See the effects on religious duties
If creatures have no power, then obligations, sending prophets, reward and punishment become futile and unjust.
4
Grasp the invalidation of Lordship and servitude
Negating God's power invalidates Lordship; negating human power invalidates servitude, as the servant cannot fulfill commands.
This completes the reductio.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Consequently, it would be said: all acts of creatures are either necessarily occurring or impossible to occur. And if the matter were so, no animal would have any power over action, because what God knew to exist is necessarily occurring, and what He knew not to issue from him is impossible to occur. Hisham argues that if God knows all acts eternally, then what He knows will happen must happen (necessary) and what He knows will not happen cannot happen (impossible). Thus all acts become either necessary or impossible. In neither case can any agent have power (qudra) over the act, since one cannot prevent the necessary nor bring about the impossible. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Hisham's argument shows that denying human power makes God's commands futile. Do you treat God's commands as futile when you excuse your disobedience by fate? What will you change today to actively obey a command you have been neglecting?
